Refuting the Philosophers on Bodily Resurrection
Al-Sawi explains three key expressions refuting the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi exposes and refutes the philosophers' denial of bodily resurrection through their flawed cosmology and Quranic allusions.

Core Idea
The philosophers err by treating some creation as eternal and mocking divine power.

In this passage, Al-Sawi unpacks the philosophers' position on bodily resurrection, showing that they arbitrarily divide the world into eternal principles and perishable branches. He then intensifies his refutation by using a Quranic phrase that threatens those who boast about their worldly achievements.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice that Al-Sawi is commenting on three distinct expressions from the original text.
The passage is concise but packed with doctrinal meaning.
2
Examine the philosophers' claim
Identify the first point: the philosophers assert that the ancient principles (uṣūl) of the world are eternal, while the branches (furūʿ) perish and never return. This underpins their denial of bodily resurrection.
Understand the distinction between uṣūl and furūʿ in their cosmology.
3
Understand the digression (bal)
Note how the word 'bal' (rather) serves as a digression (iḍrāb) to move the argument forward with a stronger refutation.
This rhetorical device signals a shift in tone.
4
Grasp the Quranic reproach
Reflect on 'kalla sawfa ya'lamūn' as a stern deterrent (radʿ wa zajr) and an allusion to the verse about mutual rivalry (al-takāthur). It warns the philosophers against their arrogance.
This connects their denial to a specific Quranic threat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (عدم المعاد الجسماني) أي: فهم يقولون : إنَّ أصول العالم القديمة لا تنعدم وفروعه تنعدم ولا تعود. قوله : (بل) فضَّلوا إضراب عما قبله قصد به الترقي في الرَّدِّ عليهم. قوله : (كلًّا سوف يعلمون كلا ردع وزجر، وفيه تعريض لهم بوعيد التكاثر .
His statement: 'the denial of bodily resurrection' means that they say the ancient principles of the world do not perish, while its branches perish and do not return. His statement: 'Rather' they preferred [them] is a digression from what preceded, intending progress in refuting them. His statement: 'No indeed, they will soon know' – 'Kalla' is a deterrent and reprimand, and in it is an allusion to the threat of mutual rivalry.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage provides commentary on three expressions from the original text regarding the philosophers' stance on bodily resurrection. First, Al-Sawi explains that their denial of bodily resurrection (ʿadam al-maʿād al-jismānī) means they claim the ancient principles (uṣūl) of the world are eternal, while the branches (furūʿ) perish without returning. Second, he comments on the phrase 'bal' (rather), which is used as a digression (iḍrāb) to intensify the refutation. Third, he explains 'kalla sawfa yaʿlamūn' (No indeed, they will soon know) as a strong deterrent and a threat against their boasting (al-takāthur).

Key Terms
عدم المعاد الجسماني
أصول العالم
فروعه
إضراب
وعيد التكاثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the philosophers only deny the return of the soul, but they deny bodily resurrection entirely.
Correction: The text clarifies that the philosophers hold that only the branches (bodies) perish without return, while principles (universals) are eternal.

Checkpoint

A) They perish entirely
B) They do not perish, but the branches perish and do not return
C) They are eternal and return after perishing
D) They are created and perish

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi refutes the philosophers' denial of bodily resurrection by exposing their flawed cosmology and invoking Quranic warning against arrogance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers assumed some parts of creation are eternal and beyond God's power. In your own mind, do you treat any part of your life—your health, wealth, or status—as if it will never end? What would it take for you to truly internalize that everything created will perish and only God remains?
?
Al-Sawi uses 'kalla' as a stern deterrent. When was the last time you heard a warning from Allah and felt personally rebuked? Are there beliefs or habits you hold that, if examined, reveal a hidden denial of divine power? Commit to identifying one such area today.
?
The philosophers' error led them to mock bodily resurrection. Do you ever find yourself mocking or minimizing aspects of the unseen (ghayb) that Allah has promised? How can you transform that skepticism into humble submission, starting with this lesson?

Refuting Deviant Views on Divine Speech
Refutation of two deviant groups on divine speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the errors of the Hanabila and Karramiyya regarding divine speech and the Sunni refutation based on the concept of self-subsistent speech.

Core Idea
Divine speech is neither an eternal accident nor created sounds; it is a self-subsistent attribute.

This lesson examines two deviant sects that misunderstood the nature of Allah's speech. The Hanabila claimed it is an eternal accident of sounds, while the Karramiyya claimed it is created letters and sounds. Sunni scholars refuted both by appealing to the reality of self-subsistent speech (kalam nafsi), which is not composed of letters or sounds yet is truly speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two deviant groups
Read the passage to find the names and their specific claims about divine speech. Note that 'Hanabila' here refers to a deviant subsect, not the followers of Imam Ahmad.
The passage explicitly distinguishes this deviant group from the orthodox Hanbalis.
2
Understand the Sunni refutation
The refutation uses the example of human self-subsistent speech, which is not letters or sounds but is still called speech. The analogy is not about perfect similarity but about sharing the property of being non-physical.
3
Distinguish the Karramiyya error
The Karramiyya claim that divine speech consists of created letters and sounds, which implies a created thing subsisting in the eternal, a problem they ignore.
The author notes that they 'do not care' about this inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحنابلة الزَّاعمين أنَّ كلامه تعالى عَرَض من جنس الأصوات والحروف، إلا أنه قديم قائم بذاته تعالى.
ردَّ عليهم أهل السُّنَّة : بأنَّ كلامنا النَّفسيَّ ليس بحرف ولا صوت وهو كلام حقيقة، وليس مراد أهل السُّنَّة التَّماثل في الحقيقة، بل التشبيه في في أنَّ كلاً منهما ليس بحرف ولا صوت وإن تباينا في الحقيقة
قوله : (والحنابلة) : المراد بهم فرقة من الفرق الضَّالَّة، وليس المراد بهم أتباع الإمام أحمد بن حنبل ؛ فإنَّهم متزَّهون عن القول بذلك. قوله : (إِلَّا أنَّه قديم قائم بذاته): راجع للحنابلة، وأما الكراميَّة فإنَّهم يقولون : إنَّ كلامه تعالى بحروف وأصوات حادثة ولا يبالون بقيام الحادث بالقديم.
And the Hanabila who claim that His speech, Most High, is an accident of the kind of sounds and letters, but it is eternal (qadim) subsisting in His essence, Most High. Ahl al-Sunna refuted them: by the fact that our self-subsistent speech (kalam nafsi) is not a letter nor a sound, yet it is truly speech. And Ahl al-Sunna do not intend similarity in reality, but resemblance in that each of them is not a letter nor a sound, even though they differ in reality. His saying: (And the Hanabila): by them is intended a sect of the deviant sects, and by them is not intended the followers of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal; for they are innocent of that saying. His saying: (But it is eternal subsisting in His essence): it refers to the Hanabila. As for the Karramiyya, they say that His speech, Most High, consists of created letters and sounds, and they do not care about the created subsisting in the eternal.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of two deviant groups: the Hanabila (a specific sect, not the followers of Imam Ahmad) and the Karramiyya. The Hanabila claim that divine speech is an accident composed of sounds and letters, but they assert it is eternal and subsists in God's essence. Ahl al-Sunna refute this by pointing to human self-subsistent speech (kalam nafsi), which is not letters or sounds yet is truly called speech. The analogy is not about complete similarity but only that both are non-physical. The Karramiyya, on the other hand, claim that divine speech consists of created letters and sounds, and they do not mind attaching created things to the eternal God. Both positions are rejected because they either attribute temporality to God or reduce His speech to a created accident. The author also clarifies that the term 'Hanabila' here refers to a deviant group, not the orthodox followers of Imam Ahmad.

عَرَض
كلام نفسي
قديم
حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'Hanabila' refers to all followers of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
Correction: The author specifies that 'Hanabila' here means a deviant sect, not the orthodox followers.

Checkpoint
What is the main error of the Karramiyya regarding divine speech according to this passage?
A) They deny that God speaks
B) They claim God's speech is an eternal accident of sounds
C) They claim God's speech consists of created letters and sounds
D) They claim God's speech is self-subsistent meaning

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position on divine speech is that it is a self-subsistent attribute, neither an eternal accident nor created sounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Hanabila and Karramiyya both fell into error by making divine speech resemble created speech. In what subtle ways might you be tempted to limit Allah's attributes by your own human experiences? Ask Allah to purify your understanding.
?
Lesson shows that Allah's speech is unlike ours. How does this affect your awe and reverence when you hear the Quran? Will you commit to reciting with more reflection on its divine origin?

God's Speech: Refuting the Hanabila and Mu'tazila
Refutes Hanabila claim and answers Mu'tazila objection on God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ahl al-Sunna refute the Hanabila claim that God's speech is an accident composed of sounds and letters, using the analogy of internal speech, and how they counter the Mu'tazila objection that internal speech is not truly called speech.

Core Idea
God's speech, like internal speech, is real but not composed of letters or sounds.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary tackles two objections about God's speech. First, the Hanabila claim that God's speech is an accident of sounds and letters yet eternal, which the Ahl al-Sunna refute by analogy with human internal speech. Second, the Mu'tazila deny that internal speech counts as speech, a claim refuted by Arabic poetry. Together, these arguments clarify the nature of God's speech as non-composite and eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the poetic citation – pay attention to its role.
2
Identify the Hanabila claim
Find where the Hanabila claim about God's speech is stated: an accident, composed of sounds/letters, yet eternal.
The claim is problematic because it mixes created and eternal attributes.
3
Understand the Ahl al-Sunna's refutation
The refutation uses human internal speech: it is not letters or sounds yet is true speech. Compare to God's speech.
Note the distinction between tamāthul (equality) and tashbīh (comparison).
4
Examine the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila deny that what one finds in oneself is speech, calling it will, knowledge, or thoughts. See how the author refutes them with Arabic usage.
The poetic line by al-Akhtal is key evidence.
5
Clarify the term 'Hanabila'
The author specifies that 'Hanabila' here means a deviant sect, not the followers of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
Also differentiate the Karamiyya position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحنابلة الزَّاعمين أنَّ كلامه تعالى عَرَض من جنس الأصوات والحروف، إلا أنه قديم قائم بذاته تعالى. ردَّ عليهم أهل السُّنَّة : بأنَّ كلامنا النَّفسيَّ ليس بحرف ولا صوت وهو كلام حقيقة، وليس مراد أهل السُّنَّة التَّماثل في الحقيقة، بل التشبيه في أنَّ كلاً منهما ليس بحرف ولا صوت وإن تباينا في الحقيقة. إن قلت : إنَّ المعتزلة ينكرون تسمية ما يجده الإنسان في نفسه كلاماً، ويردُّون ذلك للإرادة أو العلم أو الخواطر . قلت: كلامهم ساقط لمخالفته لإطلاق العرب عليه كلاماً، قال الأخطل : إنَّ الكلام لفي الفؤادِ وإِنَّما جُعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا . قوله : (والحنابلة) : المراد بهم فرقة من الفرق الضَّالَّة، وليس المراد بهم أتباع الإمام أحمد بن حنبل ؛ فإنَّهم متزَّهون عن القول بذلك. قوله : (إِلَّا أنَّه قديم قائم بذاته): راجع للحنابلة، وأما الكراميَّة فإنَّهم يقولون : إنَّ كلامه تعالى بحروف وأصوات حادثة ولا يبالون بقيام الحادث بالقديم. (١) البيت نسب للأخطل وليس في ديوانه، ونسب إلى ابن صمصام الرقاش. ينظر ذيل مرآة الزمان (۱۸۹/٣).
And the Hanabila who claim that His speech, exalted is He, is an accident of the genus of sounds and letters, except that it is eternal, subsisting in His essence. The Ahl al-Sunna refuted them: that our internal speech is not a letter nor a sound, yet it is true speech, and the Ahl al-Sunna do not intend similarity in reality, but comparison in that each of them is neither a letter nor a sound, even though they differ in reality. If you say: The Mu'tazila deny calling what a person finds in himself speech, and they refer it to will or knowledge or thoughts. I say: Their statement is invalid because it contradicts the Arabs' usage of it as speech. Al-Akhtal said: 'Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is made only a guide to the heart.' His saying: (and the Hanabila): They mean a sect of the deviant sects, not the followers of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, for they are free from such a statement. His saying: (except that it is eternal subsisting in His essence): refers to the Hanabila, but the Karamiyya say that His speech, exalted is He, consists of created letters and sounds, and they do not care about the occurrence of the contingent in the eternal. (1) The line is attributed to al-Akhtal but not in his Diwan, and attributed to Ibn Sumsam al-Ruqashi. See Dhayl Mir'at al-Zaman (189/3).
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage refutes the Hanabila sect's doctrine on God's speech and also addresses an objection from the Mu'tazila. It begins by stating the Hanabila claim: that God's speech is an accident (ʿaraḍ) composed of sounds and letters, yet eternal and subsisting in His essence. This is problematic because it suggests God's speech is like created speech. The Ahl al-Sunna respond by drawing an analogy with human internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī): our internal speech is not composed of letters or sounds, yet it is truly called speech. Similarly, God's speech is not composed of letters or sounds. However, this is not an assertion of complete similarity (tamāthul) but a comparison (tashbīh) in that both lack letters and sounds, while their realities are different. An objection is then raised: the Mu'tazila deny that internal speech is called speech; they attribute it to will, knowledge, or thoughts. The response is that such a denial contradicts the Arabs' linguistic usage, as evidenced by the poet al-Akhtal's verse: 'Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is made only a guide to the heart.' This proves that internal speech is called speech. The passage then clarifies that the term 'Hanabila' here refers to a deviant sect, not to the followers of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who are innocent of this belief. It also distinguishes the Karamiyya, who claim that God's speech consists of created letters and sounds and do not object to the occurrence of the contingent in the eternal. The footnote confirms the attribution of the poetic line.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ahl al-Sunna equate God's speech with human speech in every respect.
Correction: They only compare in that both are not letters or sounds; the realities are completely different.

D) They cite the Quran literally.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Sunna affirm that God's speech is eternal and not composed of letters or sounds, using the analogy of internal speech, and they defend calling internal speech 'speech' based on Arabic usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examples of Permissible Actions: Sending Messengers and Seeing Allah
Author gives examples of permissible actions and rebuts Mu'tazila and Hukama'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the examples of sending messengers and seeing Allah illustrate the principle that Allah's actions are purely voluntary, and how this refutes both the Mu'tazila and the philosophers.

Core Idea
Allah's actions are not bound by obligation or impossibility—they are purely voluntary.

In this passage, the commentator explains two key examples from the earlier statement: sending messengers and seeing Allah. These examples serve as concrete illustrations of the principle that Allah's actions are permissible (jā'iz), meaning He is free to do or not do them. The commentator also shows how these examples directly refute the claims of two deviant groups: the Mu'tazila, who assert obligation, and the philosophers, who assert impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the commentator links each example to a refutation of a specific group.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage mentions two examples: sending messengers and seeing Allah. For each, identify which group is refuted and what their erroneous claim is.
The Mu'tazila claim obligation; the philosophers claim impossibility.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on why these examples are considered 'permissible' actions. How do they demonstrate that Allah's actions are neither obligatory nor impossible but purely voluntary?
This is a core principle of Ash'ari theology.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Consider how this refutation of two opposing extremes (obligation vs. impossibility) protects the correct understanding of Allah's freedom and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن ذلك : بعثة الرُّسل ... إلخ) : ردَّ بذلك على المعتزلة القائلين بوجوب ،بعثتهم والحكماء القائمين باستحالتها .
قوله : ( ورؤية الباري : ردَّ به على المعتزلة القائلين بأنها محالة
His saying: 'Among that is the sending of messengers... etc.' — by that he refutes the Mu'tazila who affirm the obligation of sending them, and the philosophers (ḥukamā') who affirm its impossibility. His saying: 'And seeing the Creator' — by that he refutes the Mu'tazila who affirm that it is impossible.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (qāla) on the earlier statement that includes examples of permissible actions: sending messengers, seeing God, rewarding the disobedient, and punishing the obedient. The commentator explains that by mentioning the sending of messengers, the author refutes two opposing groups: the Mu'tazila, who claim that sending messengers is obligatory upon God, and the philosophers (ḥukamā'), who claim it is impossible. By mentioning the vision of God (ruʾya), the author refutes the Mu'tazila who assert that seeing God is impossible. Thus, these examples illustrate the principle that God's actions are purely voluntary and not bound by obligation or impossibility.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that Allah is obliged to send messengers or that sending messengers is impossible for Him.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that sending messengers is permissible (jā'iz) for Allah; He may do it or not. Both obligation and impossibility are false.

The Ash'ari-Maturidi Disagreement on Happiness and Misery
Al-Sawi defines happiness and misery and explains the verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari-Maturidi disagreement on happiness and misery and why it is considered a verbal dispute.

Core Idea
The disagreement on happiness and misery is only about terminology, not substance.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary addresses a classic point of divergence between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. While they appear to disagree on whether happiness and misery can change, al-Sawi explains that the difference is merely verbal, as both agree that the final end is what truly matters. Let's explore the definitions and the positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to grasp the basic definitions of happiness and misery according to both schools.
Notice how al-Sawi defines terms before presenting the disagreement.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the Ash'ari position (no change) and the Maturidi position (change possible). Note how each school uses different terminology.
The Ash'ari view sees happiness/misery as eternal; the Maturidi view sees them as changeable based on the final end.
3
Understand the verbal dispute
Reflect on al-Sawi's statement that the dispute is verbal. Both schools agree that the decisive factor is the final state (khatima) at death. The only difference is in how they label the relationship.
This clarifies that there is no real theological conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السعادة والشقاوة عند الأشاعرة والماتريدية
من مات على الكفر أو الإيمان، وعند الماتريدي هو الكافر أو المؤمن .
وينبني على هذا الخلاف هل الشَّقاوة والسَّعادة يتبدَّلان؟ فقال الأوَّل : لا ، والثاني: نعم، والخُلف لفظي .
قوله : (من مات على الكفر أو على الإيمان) : ل ونشر مرتّب .
قوله : (فقال الأوَّل) : أي : وهو الأشعري. وقوله : (لا) : أي : لا يتبدلان بل هما أزليَّتان، والإسلام والكفر علامة السَّعادة والشَّقاوة .
قوله : (والثاني) : أي : وهو الماتريدي. وقوله : (نعم) : أي : يتبدلان ، فإذا مات المسلم على الكفر؛ فقد انقلبت سعادته شقاوة، وإذا أسلم الكافر عند الموت ؛ فقد انقلبت شقاوته سعادة.
قوله : (والخلف لفظيّ) : أي : لأنَّ العبرة بالخاتمة على كلا القولين، وإِنَّما الخلاف في التّسمية فقط، فالأشاعرة يقولون : الإسلام علامة على السعادة لأنفسها، والكفر علامة على الشقاوة لأنفسها
Happiness and misery according to the Ash'aris and the Maturidis: whoever dies upon disbelief or faith, and according to al-Maturidi it is the disbeliever or the believer. And upon this difference is based the question: do misery and happiness change? So the first said: No, and the second: Yes, and the dispute is verbal. His saying: (whoever dies upon disbelief or upon faith): [unclear]. His saying: (so the first said): meaning al-Ash'ari. And his saying: (No): meaning they do not change, rather they are eternal, and Islam and disbelief are the sign of happiness and misery. His saying: (and the second): meaning al-Maturidi. And his saying: (Yes): meaning they change, so if a Muslim dies upon disbelief, his happiness has turned into misery, and if a disbeliever becomes Muslim at death, his misery has turned into happiness. His saying: (and the dispute is verbal): meaning because the consideration is the final end according to both sayings, and the disagreement is only in terminology, so the Ash'aris say: Islam is a sign of happiness in itself, and disbelief is a sign of misery in itself.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage defines the terms happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa) and explains the point of disagreement between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. Al-Sawi begins by stating the basic definitions: according to the Ash'aris, happiness and misery are what result from dying upon faith or disbelief respectively, while according to al-Maturidi, the terms refer to the person themselves. From this, the question arises: Can happiness and misery change? The Ash'ari answers no, they are eternal; the Maturidi answers yes, they can change based on the final end. However, al-Sawi immediately clarifies that this dispute is merely verbal (lafzi). Both schools agree that the decisive factor is the final state (khatima) at death. The only difference is in how they label the relationship: Ash'aris say that Islam/disbelief is a sign (alama) of an underlying eternal happiness/misery, implying that the happiness/misery itself does not change. The Maturidis, by using different terminology, allow for the possibility that a person's final state can reverse their previous classification. Despite this terminological difference, both schools ultimately agree that salvation depends on the end of one's life.

Key Terms
السعادة
الشقاوة
الخلف اللفظي
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari and Maturidi have a real substantive disagreement on whether happiness and misery are fixed or changeable.
Correction: Al-Sawi explains that the disagreement is only verbal; both schools agree that the decisive factor is the final state at death.

Checkpoint

A) Substantive
B) Verbal
C) Foundational
D) Irresolvable

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari-Maturidi disagreement on happiness and misery is a verbal dispute; both agree that the final end determines one's ultimate state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you relying on your current state of faith as a guarantee, or do you live with genuine concern for how you will end? What would change in your daily actions if you truly believed your final state could flip unexpectedly?
?
Al-Sawi says the dispute is only verbal, yet both schools emphasize the final end. Does your heart tremble at the uncertainty of your khatima, or do you assume it will be good? What concrete step will you take this week to plead with Allah for a good ending?
?
If happiness and misery are ultimately determined by Allah's decree and only signaled by one's final actions, where does your sense of control come from? How does this truth humble your pride in your own deeds?

Rhetorical Analysis and the Meaning of Wujub
Al-Sawi analyzes the figure of speech and defines the type of obligation relevant to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi uses rhetorical analysis to refute the Mu'tazilite view and the specific meaning of wujub applicable to God.

Core Idea
Al-Sawi's metaphor exposes the Mu'tazilites' failure in adab, and clarifies that obligation upon God cannot imply subjugation.

Al-Sawi unpacks the metaphor in the claim 'the Mu'tazilites have mistreated good manners' to show the gravity of their error. He then argues that obligation upon God cannot be the type that entails blame, because that would imply subjugation. This lesson connects rhetorical analysis with theological precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own rhythm and terms come through.
Pay attention to the parentheses—they are textual notes from the commentator.
2
Understand the metaphor structure
Identify the two types of isti'ara: kinaya in 'adab' and takhili in 'asa'a'. Notice how the phrase works as a whole kinaya for lack of adab.
The passage explains that the metaphor implies distance and aversion, which is more eloquent than literal statement.
3
Grasp the logical implication
Al-Sawi states that obligation (wujub) implies subjugation (qahr). If something is obligatory upon God, it would mean He is subjugated, which is impossible.
This is the core theological argument: the Mu'tazilite claim of obligation upon God leads to an impossibility.
4
Recognize the distinction of wujub
The passage clarifies that the obligation intended cannot be the legislative type (shar'i) that entails blame for omission. Instead, it must be a different kind that does not imply subjugation.
This distinction is crucial for understanding how kalam preserves divine transcendence.
5
Reflect on rhetoric serving theology
Notice how the rhetorical analysis (metaphor) sets up the theological conclusion. The vivid metaphor of mistreatment reinforces the gravity of the error before the logical argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم القول بالصلاح والأصلح في حقه تعالى، وهم المعتزلة، قد أساء الأدبا (حذف الفاء ضرورة) أي فقد أحزن الأدبا اللائق بحقه تعالى والألف للإطلاق أيضاً. ففي الأدب استعارة بالكناية، وفي الإساءة استعارة تخييلية، ثم الكلام كناية عن عدم اتصافهم بالأدب؛ لأنه يلزم من إساءتك لغيرك بعده عنك ونفرته منك، بل لا يستطيع أن ينظر إليك، وهي أبلغ من الحقيقة؛ يعني أنهم أخلوا بالأدب مع الله تعالى غاية الإخلال، حتى خلت قلوبهم عن بوارق الإجلال، وارتكبوا بدعة شنيعة وقوة فظيعة، وذلك لأن من وجب عليه شيء فهو مقهور. ثم لا يصح أن يراد بالوجوب عليه تعالى ما يستحقه تاركه من الذم والعقاب. قوله : (حذف الفاء ضرورة) أي ولولا الضرورة لوجب اقتران الجملة بالفاء لتصديرها بـ (قد). قوله : (استعارة بالكناية) أي فقد شبه الأدب بإنسان أحزنه شخص، وطوى ذكر المشبه به ورمز له بشيء من لوازمه وهو الإساءة، فإثباتها تخييل. قوله : (وهي) أي الكناية. قوله : (عن بوارق الإجلال) أي عن أنوار التعظيم والاحترام. قوله : (وذلك) أي وبيان الدليل على وجوب عدم وجوب الصلاح والأصلح. قوله : (ما يستحقه تاركه الذم والعقاب) أي وهو الوجوب الشرعي.
The ruling on the claim of what is good and more beneficial in His right, exalted, and they are the Mu'tazilites, he has mistreated good manners (due to poetic necessity) i.e., he has grieved, the proper good manners due to His right, exalted, and the alif is for release also. For in (the word) 'adab' there is a metaphor by way of kinaya, and in (the word) 'asa'a' there is an imaginative metaphor. Then the speech is a kinaya about their not being characterized by good manners, because it follows from your mistreating someone that he becomes distant from you and averse to you, rather he cannot even look at you, and it is more eloquent than the literal expression. It means that they have failed in good manners with God, exalted, to the utmost failure, until their hearts are empty of the lightning flashes of majesty, and they have committed a heretical innovation and a terrible power. And that is because whoever has something obligatory upon him is subjugated. Then it is not correct to intend by obligation upon Him, exalted, that for which the doer of it deserves blame and punishment. His saying: (the deletion of the fa' due to poetic necessity) means: if not for necessity, it would be obligatory to connect the sentence with the fa', because it begins with 'qad'. His saying: (metaphor by way of kinaya) means: he likened good manners to a person whom someone grieved, and he omitted mentioning the likened-to and symbolised it with something from its necessary concomitants, which is mistreatment, so its affirmation is imaginative. His saying: (and it) refers to the kinaya. His saying: (from the lightning flashes of majesty) means from the lights of glorification and respect. His saying: (and that) means the clarification of the proof of the obligation of non-obligation of what is good and more beneficial. His saying: (that for which the doer of it deserves blame and punishment) means it is the legislative obligation.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a comment on the phrase 'the Mu'tazilites have mistreated good manners' from the matn. The author provides a rhetorical analysis of the metaphor used. He explains that the phrase employs two types of isti'ara: kinaya (allusion) in the word 'adab' and takhili (imaginative) in the word 'asa'a'. The overall expression is a kinaya for the Mu'tazilites' utter lack of proper adab with God. The author then explains the logic: mistreating someone leads to distance and aversion, which is a powerful way to express the gravity of their error. He then connects this to the concept of wujub (obligation): to say something is obligatory upon God would imply that God is subjugated (maqhur), which is impossible. Therefore, the obligation referred to cannot be the moral or legal obligation that entails blame for omission; rather, it must be a different kind of wujub. The commentary concludes by defining the type of wujub applicable to God: it is not the shar'i (legislative) obligation that implies blame for omission. The subsequent lines explain specific textual notes: the deletion of the fa' due to poetic necessity, and the metaphor details.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obligation upon God is the same as legal/moral obligation on humans, implying blame for omission.
Correction: Obligation upon God does not entail blame or subjugation; it must be understood in a different sense.

Checkpoint
What kind of obligation is not applicable to God according to the passage?
A) Rational obligation.
B) Legislative obligation that entails blame for omission.
C) Natural obligation.
D) Moral obligation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi uses rhetorical analysis to show the Mu'tazilites' error and clarifies that wujub for God does not entail blame or subjugation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that the Mu'tazilites' error began with a lack of adab toward Allah. Reflect: In what areas of your life do you find yourself demanding that Allah act according to your sense of fairness or expectation? How does that demand reveal a subtle lack of adab? Will you today repent from that attitude and submit to His absolute wisdom?
?
The metaphor of mistreating manners leads to distance from the one mistreated. Consider a period when you felt distant from Allah. Can you trace that distance to a specific instance where you insisted on your own 'right' or understanding over His command? Are you ready to let go of that insistence to restore closeness?
?
Al-Sawi clarifies that any obligation on God implying subjugation is impossible. Yet, human hearts often hold Allah accountable to their own standards. Identify one grievance you have held against Allah silently. How does this lesson challenge that grievance? What step can you take today to replace that grievance with trust in His perfection?

Rhetorical Condemnation of the Mu'tazilites
Poetic line interpreted rhetorically to condemn Mu'tazilites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses rhetorical devices (istiʿāra, kināya) to condemn the Mu'tazilite view that God is obliged to do what is best.

Core Idea
Rhetoric exposes the theological error of ascribing obligation to God.

The passage begins by interpreting a poetic line that accuses the Mu'tazilites of committing a breach of propriety towards God. The author unpacks the metaphor and metonymy to show how severe their error is. This rhetorical analysis sets the tone for the theological refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line and its explanation
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'قد أساءوا الأدبا' and its commentary. Notice how the author explains the omission of the letter fā' and the use of figurative language.
The line is a condensed accusation; the commentary unpacks it.
2
Identify the two types of metaphor
Look for the terms 'istiʿāra bil-kināya' and 'istiʿāra takhyīliyya'. Understand how they work together to convey the meaning.
The author explains that 'adab' is likened to a person, and 'isā'a' is its imaginative concomitant.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that the Mu'tazilites failed in proper conduct to the utmost, and that this is because one upon whom something is obligatory is subdued. This sets up the next argument about obligation.
The link to obligation (wujūb) is the key transition.

حكم القول بالصلاح والأصلح في حقه تعالى، وهم المعتزلة، قد أساء) حذف الفاء ضرورة؛ أي فقد أحزن (الأدبا) اللائق بحقه تعالى والألف للإطلاق أيضاً. ففي الأدب استعارة بالكناية، وفي الإساءة استعارة تخييلية، ثمَّ الكلام كناية عن عدم اتصافهم بالأدب ؛ لأنَّه يلزم من إساءتك لغيرك بعده عنك، ونفرته منك، بل لا يستطيع أن ينظر إليك، وهي أبلغ من الحقيقة؛ يعني : أنَّهم أخلُّوا بالأدب مع الله تعالى غاية الإخلال، حتى خَلَت قلوبهم عن بوارق الإجلال، وارتكبوا بدعة شنيعة وقوة فظيعة، وذلك لأنَّ من وجب عليه شيء فهو مقهور. ثمَّ لا يصح أن يراد بالوجوب عليه تعالى ما يستحقُّ تاركه الذَّمَّ والعقاب ... قوله : (حذف الفاء ضرورة :أي ولولا الضرورة لوجب اقتران الجملة بالفاء ؛ لتصديرها بـ (قد). قوله : (استعارة بالكناية :أي: فقد شبَّه الأدب بإنسان أحزنه شخص، وطوى ذكر المشبه به ورمز له بشيء من لوازمه؛ وهو الإساءة، فإثباتها تخييل . قوله : (وهي) : أي : الكناية. قوله : (عن بوارق الإجلال :أي عن أنوار التعظيم والاحترام. قوله : (وذلك ) : أي : وبيان الدَّليل على وجوب عدم وجوب الصلاح والأصلح. حاشية الصاوي على شرح الخريدة البهية كما في حق المكلَّفين، وهو ظاهر، فما بقي إلا أنَّ معناه لزوم صدور الأصلح عنه، بحيث لا يتمكن من التّرك ، وإلا فلا معنى للوجوب. وأقوى ما تمسكوا به في ذلك : أنَّ تَرْك الأصلح يستلزم المحال؛ سفه أو جهل أو عبث أو بخل، وظاهر أنه رفض لقاعدة الاختيار، وتمسك بالفلسفة الظاهرة العوار . وحُكي أنَّ أبا الحسن الأشعري رضي الله عنه سأل شيخه أبا هاشم الجبائي رحمه الله عند - وهو يقرّر مسألة وجوب الصَّلاح - فقال له: ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة، مات أحدهم مطيعاً، والآخر عاصياً، والثالث صغيراً؟ فقال : الأوَّل يثاب في الجنَّة، والثَّاني يعاقب في النَّار، والثَّالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب . فقال الأشعري : فإن قال الثَّالث : يا ربِّ ؛ لم أمتني صغيراً، ولم تبقني إلى أن أكبر فأطيعك لأثاب في الجنَّة؟ فقال الجبّائي : يقول الرَّبُّ تعالى : إِنِّي كنت أعلم منك أنَّك لو كبرت لعصيت فدخلت النَّار، فكان الأصلح لك موتك صغيراً . فقال الأشعري : لا فإن قال الثَّاني : يا ربِّ ؛ لِمَ لَمْ تمتني صغيراً لئلا أعصي فأدخل النَّار؟ فماذا يقول الرَّبُّ؟ أنَّه فَبهت الجُبَّائي، ويروى قال للأشعري : أَبكَ جنون؟ فقال الأشعري : لا ولكن وقف حمار الشيخ في العقبة . فترك الأشعري مذهبه واشتغل هو ومن معه بإبطال رأي المعتزلة، وإثبات ما وردت به السُّنَّة ومضى عليه الجماعة، فسُمُّوا أهل السُّنَّة والجماعة .
The ruling on the statement concerning al-salāh wa al-aslah (what is best and most beneficial) in His right, exalted is He, and they are the Mu'tazila, (they) have committed a breach of propriety) the fā' was omitted due to necessity; i.e., they have saddened (the proper conduct) befitting His right, exalted is He, and the alif is also for absolute release. For in 'proper conduct' there is a metaphorical allusion (istiʿāra bil-kināya), and in 'breach of propriety' there is an imaginative metaphor (istiʿāra takhyīliyya), then the speech is a metonymy (kināya) for their lack of proper conduct; because it follows from your breach of propriety towards another that he distances himself from you, and shuns you, rather he cannot look at you, and it is more eloquent than the literal truth; meaning: that they have failed in proper conduct with God, exalted is He, to the utmost failure, until their hearts became empty of the flashes of veneration, and they committed an ugly innovation and a terrible force, and that is because one upon whom something is obligatory is subdued. Then it is not correct that what is intended by obligation (wujūb) upon Him, exalted is He, is that which its abandoner deserves blame and punishment... His saying: (the fā' was omitted due to necessity: i.e., were it not for necessity, the sentence would have to be connected with the fā', because it begins with (qad). His saying: (metaphorical allusion: i.e., he likened proper conduct to a person whom someone saddened, and suppressed the mention of the likened-to and alluded to it by something from its concomitants; namely, breach of propriety, so its affirmation is imaginative. His saying: (it): i.e., the metonymy. His saying: (from the flashes of veneration: i.e., from the lights of magnification and reverence. His saying: (and that: i.e., and the explanation of the proof for the obligation of the non-obligation of al-salāh and al-aslah. Al-Ṣāwī's gloss on the commentary of al-Kharīdat al-Bahiyya: as in the case of the legally responsible, and it is apparent, so nothing remains except that its meaning is the necessity of the occurrence of al-aslah from Him, such that He is not able to refrain, and if not, then there is no meaning to obligation. And the strongest thing they adhered to in that is that abandoning al-aslah entails the impossible: foolishness or ignorance or futility or stinginess, and it is apparent that it is a rejection of the principle of choice, and an adherence to a philosophy whose defect is apparent. And it is narrated that Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, may God be pleased with him, asked his teacher Abū Hāshim al-Jubbāʾī, may God have mercy on him, when he was expounding the issue of the obligation of al-ṣalāḥ, and said to him: What do you say about three brothers, one of whom died obedient, the other disobedient, and the third a child? He said: The first is rewarded in Paradise, the second is punished in Hell, and the third is neither rewarded nor punished. Al-Ashʿarī said: Then if the third says: O Lord, why did You cause me to die as a child, and not keep me alive until I grew up so that I could obey You and be rewarded in Paradise? Al-Jubbāʾī said: The Lord, exalted is He, says: I knew that if you grew up you would disobey and enter Hell, so what was best (al-aṣlaḥ) for you was your dying as a child. Al-Ashʿarī said: No, if the second says: O Lord, why did You not cause me to die as a child so that I would not disobey and enter Hell? So what does the Lord say? And al-Jubbāʾī was confounded, and it is narrated that he said to al-Ashʿarī: Are you mad? Al-Ashʿarī said: No, but the donkey of the shaykh has stopped at the hill. So al-Ashʿarī left his school and occupied himself, he and those with him, with refuting the opinion of the Muʿtazila, and establishing what the Sunna came with and what the community proceeded upon, so they were called the People of the Sunna and the Community.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God is obliged (wājib) to do what is best and most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His creatures. The author begins by explaining a line of poetry that condemns the Mu'tazilites for committing a breach of propriety (asā'ū al-adab) towards God. He analyzes the rhetorical devices (istiʿāra, kināya) in the line to emphasize the severity of their error. Then he argues that the concept of obligation (wujūb) upon God cannot mean the same as in human legal obligation (what deserves blame if abandoned), because God cannot be coerced. Instead, the Mu'tazilite position implies that God's actions are necessitated, which contradicts His sovereign will and power. The strongest argument they present is that if God did not do the most beneficial, it would imply foolishness, ignorance, futility, or stinginess. The author dismisses this as a rejection of divine choice and a reliance on defective philosophy. Then he narrates the famous story of al-Ashʿarī debating his Mu'tazilite teacher al-Jubbāʾī. Al-Ashʿarī asks about three brothers: one dies obedient, one disobedient, and one as a child. Al-Jubbāʾī answers that the obedient is rewarded, the disobedient punished, and the child is neutral. Al-Ashʿarī then poses a dilemma: if the child asks why he was not kept alive to obey, al-Jubbāʾī says God knew he would disobey if grown. But then al-Ashʿarī asks: if the disobedient brother asks why he was not died as a child to avoid sin, God's answer cannot be consistent. Al-Jubbāʾī is confounded. This story serves as a decisive refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine, showing it leads to absurdities. After this, al-Ashʿarī abandoned the Mu'tazilite school and dedicated himself to establishing Sunni doctrine based on the Sunna and the consensus of the early community, thus founding the Ashʿarī school.

Key Terms
الصلاح والأصلح (al-salāh wa al-aslah)
الوجوب (wujūb)
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)
استعارة (istiʿāra)
كناية (kināya)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-Aslah is so flawed that it constitutes a breach of propriety towards God, as expressed through powerful rhetoric.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever entertained a belief about Allah that, if logically extended, would impose an obligation on Him? How does this passage challenge you to examine the hidden implications of your own theological assumptions? What will you do to guard against such subtle breaches of adab in your understanding of God?
?
In what ways do you, like the Mu'tazilites, risk reducing the Almighty to a being who must act according to your sense of fairness or what you consider 'best'? Where in your heart might a demand for justice obscure the reverence due to His absolute will? Commit today to replacing any 'must' with humble submission to His decree.
?
This passage teaches that even a seemingly noble doctrine can be a profound failure of adab toward God. Are there any practices or beliefs you hold dear that, upon reflection, might be rooted more in human reasoning than in awe of Allah? What specific step can you take this week to purify your intention and align your thought with His majesty?

Why Obligation Cannot Apply to God
Rational argument against obligation on God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that obligation (wujūb) cannot be attributed to God, and that the Mu'tazilite claim that God must do what is best leads to absurdities.

Core Idea
God cannot be obliged; obligation implies a subduer and is incompatible with divine sovereignty.

After the rhetorical condemnation, the author provides a rational argument. He first clarifies that the Mu'tazilite concept of obligation on God cannot mean deserving blame for omission. Then he presents and dismisses their strongest proof—that not doing the best would imply defects in God. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the logical argument
Read the passage from 'ثمَّ لا يصح أن يراد بالوجوب' to 'تمسك بالفلسفة الظاهرة العوار'. Focus on the author's reasoning.
The argument is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two key moves
First, the author negates that obligation on God means what it means for humans. Second, he refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that abandoning al-aslah entails foolishness, ignorance, etc.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Notice that the author dismisses the Mu'tazilite argument as a rejection of divine choice and a reliance on defective philosophy. This is a high-level refutation.
The term 'قاعدة الاختيار' (principle of choice) is central.

حكم القول بالصلاح والأصلح في حقه تعالى، وهم المعتزلة، قد أساء) حذف الفاء ضرورة؛ أي فقد أحزن (الأدبا) اللائق بحقه تعالى والألف للإطلاق أيضاً. ففي الأدب استعارة بالكناية، وفي الإساءة استعارة تخييلية، ثمَّ الكلام كناية عن عدم اتصافهم بالأدب ؛ لأنَّه يلزم من إساءتك لغيرك بعده عنك، ونفرته منك، بل لا يستطيع أن ينظر إليك، وهي أبلغ من الحقيقة؛ يعني : أنَّهم أخلُّوا بالأدب مع الله تعالى غاية الإخلال، حتى خَلَت قلوبهم عن بوارق الإجلال، وارتكبوا بدعة شنيعة وقوة فظيعة، وذلك لأنَّ من وجب عليه شيء فهو مقهور. ثمَّ لا يصح أن يراد بالوجوب عليه تعالى ما يستحقُّ تاركه الذَّمَّ والعقاب ... قوله : (حذف الفاء ضرورة :أي ولولا الضرورة لوجب اقتران الجملة بالفاء ؛ لتصديرها بـ (قد). قوله : (استعارة بالكناية :أي: فقد شبَّه الأدب بإنسان أحزنه شخص، وطوى ذكر المشبه به ورمز له بشيء من لوازمه؛ وهو الإساءة، فإثباتها تخييل . قوله : (وهي) : أي : الكناية. قوله : (عن بوارق الإجلال :أي عن أنوار التعظيم والاحترام. قوله : (وذلك ) : أي : وبيان الدَّليل على وجوب عدم وجوب الصلاح والأصلح. حاشية الصاوي على شرح الخريدة البهية كما في حق المكلَّفين، وهو ظاهر، فما بقي إلا أنَّ معناه لزوم صدور الأصلح عنه، بحيث لا يتمكن من التّرك ، وإلا فلا معنى للوجوب. وأقوى ما تمسكوا به في ذلك : أنَّ تَرْك الأصلح يستلزم المحال؛ سفه أو جهل أو عبث أو بخل، وظاهر أنه رفض لقاعدة الاختيار، وتمسك بالفلسفة الظاهرة العوار . وحُكي أنَّ أبا الحسن الأشعري رضي الله عنه سأل شيخه أبا هاشم الجبائي رحمه الله عند - وهو يقرّر مسألة وجوب الصَّلاح - فقال له: ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة، مات أحدهم مطيعاً، والآخر عاصياً، والثالث صغيراً؟ فقال : الأوَّل يثاب في الجنَّة، والثَّاني يعاقب في النَّار، والثَّالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب . فقال الأشعري : فإن قال الثَّالث : يا ربِّ ؛ لم أمتني صغيراً، ولم تبقني إلى أن أكبر فأطيعك لأثاب في الجنَّة؟ فقال الجبّائي : يقول الرَّبُّ تعالى : إِنِّي كنت أعلم منك أنَّك لو كبرت لعصيت فدخلت النَّار، فكان الأصلح لك موتك صغيراً . فقال الأشعري : لا فإن قال الثَّاني : يا ربِّ ؛ لِمَ لَمْ تمتني صغيراً لئلا أعصي فأدخل النَّار؟ فماذا يقول الرَّبُّ؟ أنَّه فَبهت الجُبَّائي، ويروى قال للأشعري : أَبكَ جنون؟ فقال الأشعري : لا ولكن وقف حمار الشيخ في العقبة . فترك الأشعري مذهبه واشتغل هو ومن معه بإبطال رأي المعتزلة، وإثبات ما وردت به السُّنَّة ومضى عليه الجماعة، فسُمُّوا أهل السُّنَّة والجماعة .
The ruling on the statement concerning al-salāh wa al-aslah (what is best and most beneficial) in His right, exalted is He, and they are the Mu'tazila, (they) have committed a breach of propriety) the fā' was omitted due to necessity; i.e., they have saddened (the proper conduct) befitting His right, exalted is He, and the alif is also for absolute release. For in 'proper conduct' there is a metaphorical allusion (istiʿāra bil-kināya), and in 'breach of propriety' there is an imaginative metaphor (istiʿāra takhyīliyya), then the speech is a metonymy (kināya) for their lack of proper conduct; because it follows from your breach of propriety towards another that he distances himself from you, and shuns you, rather he cannot look at you, and it is more eloquent than the literal truth; meaning: that they have failed in proper conduct with God, exalted is He, to the utmost failure, until their hearts became empty of the flashes of veneration, and they committed an ugly innovation and a terrible force, and that is because one upon whom something is obligatory is subdued. Then it is not correct that what is intended by obligation (wujūb) upon Him, exalted is He, is that which its abandoner deserves blame and punishment... His saying: (the fā' was omitted due to necessity: i.e., were it not for necessity, the sentence would have to be connected with the fā', because it begins with (qad). His saying: (metaphorical allusion: i.e., he likened proper conduct to a person whom someone saddened, and suppressed the mention of the likened-to and alluded to it by something from its concomitants; namely, breach of propriety, so its affirmation is imaginative. His saying: (it): i.e., the metonymy. His saying: (from the flashes of veneration: i.e., from the lights of magnification and reverence. His saying: (and that: i.e., and the explanation of the proof for the obligation of the non-obligation of al-salāh and al-aslah. Al-Ṣāwī's gloss on the commentary of al-Kharīdat al-Bahiyya: as in the case of the legally responsible, and it is apparent, so nothing remains except that its meaning is the necessity of the occurrence of al-aslah from Him, such that He is not able to refrain, and if not, then there is no meaning to obligation. And the strongest thing they adhered to in that is that abandoning al-aslah entails the impossible: foolishness or ignorance or futility or stinginess, and it is apparent that it is a rejection of the principle of choice, and an adherence to a philosophy whose defect is apparent. And it is narrated that Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, may God be pleased with him, asked his teacher Abū Hāshim al-Jubbāʾī, may God have mercy on him, when he was expounding the issue of the obligation of al-ṣalāḥ, and said to him: What do you say about three brothers, one of whom died obedient, the other disobedient, and the third a child? He said: The first is rewarded in Paradise, the second is punished in Hell, and the third is neither rewarded nor punished. Al-Ashʿarī said: Then if the third says: O Lord, why did You cause me to die as a child, and not keep me alive until I grew up so that I could obey You and be rewarded in Paradise? Al-Jubbāʾī said: The Lord, exalted is He, says: I knew that if you grew up you would disobey and enter Hell, so what was best (al-aṣlaḥ) for you was your dying as a child. Al-Ashʿarī said: No, if the second says: O Lord, why did You not cause me to die as a child so that I would not disobey and enter Hell? So what does the Lord say? And al-Jubbāʾī was confounded, and it is narrated that he said to al-Ashʿarī: Are you mad? Al-Ashʿarī said: No, but the donkey of the shaykh has stopped at the hill. So al-Ashʿarī left his school and occupied himself, he and those with him, with refuting the opinion of the Muʿtazila, and establishing what the Sunna came with and what the community proceeded upon, so they were called the People of the Sunna and the Community.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God is obliged (wājib) to do what is best and most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His creatures. The author begins by explaining a line of poetry that condemns the Mu'tazilites for committing a breach of propriety (asā'ū al-adab) towards God. He analyzes the rhetorical devices (istiʿāra, kināya) in the line to emphasize the severity of their error. Then he argues that the concept of obligation (wujūb) upon God cannot mean the same as in human legal obligation (what deserves blame if abandoned), because God cannot be coerced. Instead, the Mu'tazilite position implies that God's actions are necessitated, which contradicts His sovereign will and power. The strongest argument they present is that if God did not do the most beneficial, it would imply foolishness, ignorance, futility, or stinginess. The author dismisses this as a rejection of divine choice and a reliance on defective philosophy. Then he narrates the famous story of al-Ashʿarī debating his Mu'tazilite teacher al-Jubbāʾī. Al-Ashʿarī asks about three brothers: one dies obedient, one disobedient, and one as a child. Al-Jubbāʾī answers that the obedient is rewarded, the disobedient punished, and the child is neutral. Al-Ashʿarī then poses a dilemma: if the child asks why he was not kept alive to obey, al-Jubbāʾī says God knew he would disobey if grown. But then al-Ashʿarī asks: if the disobedient brother asks why he was not died as a child to avoid sin, God's answer cannot be consistent. Al-Jubbāʾī is confounded. This story serves as a decisive refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine, showing it leads to absurdities. After this, al-Ashʿarī abandoned the Mu'tazilite school and dedicated himself to establishing Sunni doctrine based on the Sunna and the consensus of the early community, thus founding the Ashʿarī school.

Key Terms
الوجوب (wujūb)
الصلاح والأصلح (al-salāh wa al-aslah)
قاعدة الاختيار (qāʿidat al-ikhtiyār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God does not do the best for His creatures, it indicates a defect in God.
Correction: The author argues that God's actions are not subject to human standards of benefit; He chooses freely, and what He does is always wise and good.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the strongest Mu'tazilite argument for obligation?
A) That God's mercy requires it
B) That abandoning al-aslah entails foolishness, ignorance, or stinginess
C) That the Prophet said so
D) That reason alone proves it

Key Takeaway
Obligation on God is impossible because it implies coercion; the Mu'tazilite argument from possible defects is a rejection of divine choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your prayer, do you ever catch yourself expecting God to answer a certain way because you have been 'good enough'? How does the impossibility of obligation on God reframe that expectation into submission (islām) rather than demand?
?
When life disappoints you and you feel God has not given you what you 'needed', whose standard of benefit are you measuring by? Will you trust His choice even when it contradicts your calculation of what is best for you?

The Story of the Three Brothers
Narrative refutation via the story of three brothers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the famous story of the three brothers decisively refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-Aslah and leads to the founding of the Ashʿarī school.

Core Idea
A simple dilemma exposes the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position.

The most famous part of this section is the story of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari debating his Mu'tazilite teacher al-Jubba'i. Through a clever question about three brothers, al-Ash'ari reveals an inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is best. This lesson tells the story and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the passage starting from 'وحُكي أنَّ أبا الحسن الأشعري' to the end. Follow the dialogue carefully.
Pay attention to the questions and answers.
2
Identify the dilemma
The key is that al-Jubba'i's answer to the third brother creates a contradiction when applied to the second brother. Work through the logic.
3
Understand the outcome
Notice that al-Jubba'i cannot answer and resorts to insulting al-Ash'ari. Then al-Ash'ari leaves the Mu'tazilite school and founds the Sunni school.
This story is a turning point in Islamic theology.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God is obliged (wājib) to do what is best and most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His creatures. The author begins by explaining a line of poetry that condemns the Mu'tazilites for committing a breach of propriety (asā'ū al-adab) towards God. Then he argues that the concept of obligation (wujūb) upon God cannot mean the same as in human legal obligation (what deserves blame if abandoned), because God cannot be coerced. Instead, the Mu'tazilite position implies that God's actions are necessitated, which contradicts His sovereign will and power. The strongest argument they present is that if God did not do the most beneficial, it would imply foolishness, ignorance, futility, or stinginess. The author dismisses this as a rejection of divine choice and a reliance on defective philosophy. Then he narrates the famous story of al-Ashʿarī debating his Mu'tazilite teacher al-Jubbāʾī. Al-Ashʿarī asks about three brothers: one dies obedient, one disobedient, and one as a child. Al-Jubbāʾī answers that the obedient is rewarded, the disobedient punished, and the child is neutral. Al-Ashʿarī then poses a dilemma: if the child asks why he was not kept alive to obey, al-Jubbāʾī says God knew he would disobey if grown. But then al-Ashʿarī asks: if the disobedient brother asks why he was not died as a child to avoid sin, God's answer cannot be consistent. Al-Jubbāʾī is confounded. Key Terms
أبو الحسن الأشعري (Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī)
أبو هاشم الجبائي (Abū Hāshim al-Jubbāʾī)
الصلاح والأصلح (al-salāh wa al-aslah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the story shows that God acts arbitrarily or without wisdom.
Correction: The story actually refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obliged to do the best for each individual; it does not deny divine wisdom but affirms that God's actions are not subject to human calculations of benefit.

Checkpoint
Why was al-Jubba'i confounded by al-Ash'ari's second question?
A) He realized his doctrine was wrong
B) He could not give a consistent answer for the second brother
C) He forgot his own theology
D) He was insulted by al-Ash'ari

Key Takeaway
The story of the three brothers demonstrates the incoherence of the Mu'tazilite doctrine and marks the birth of the Ashʿarī school.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a dilemma that challenges your deeply held beliefs, do you react like al-Jubba'i by dismissing the questioner or like al-Ash'ari by sincerely seeking the truth? What would it take for you to abandon a comfortable position for the sake of sound thinking?
?
The child in the story could not act, yet God's knowledge was used to decide his fate. How does this challenge your assumption about your own responsibility and God's justice? Are you willing to accept that your choices are not the final measure of God's wisdom?
?
Al-Ash'ari left his teacher after this exchange despite years of loyalty. Is there a theological stance you hold that you have inherited without questioning? What specific step could you take this week to examine whether it truly aligns with the Quran and Sunnah?

Origin of the Name Mu'tazila
Etymology of the name Mu'tazila from a study circle incident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific historical event that led to the naming of the Mu'tazila and the doctrine of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila were named after Wāṣil ibn 'Aṭā' withdrew from al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī's study circle.

This lesson explores the origin of the name 'Mu'tazila' (the Withdrawers). It recounts a key event in early Islamic theological history when Wāṣil ibn 'Aṭā' proposed a new doctrine about the status of grave sinners. This event not only gave the sect its name but also defined its distinctive theological stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source and its English translation side by side. Pay attention to the key terms and the flow of the narrative.
The passage is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the key figures and the event
Note that Wāṣil ibn 'Aṭā' was a student of al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī. The event takes place in al-Ḥasan's study circle, where Wāṣil presents a new theological opinion.
3
Understand the doctrine of the intermediate position
Wāṣil's doctrine states that a committer of a major sin (kabīra) is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but in an intermediate station. This is the core theological reason for the withdrawal.
This doctrine is known as al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn.
4
Connect the name to the event
The name 'Mu'tazila' comes from the verb iʿtazala (to withdraw). Al-Ḥasan's statement 'Wāṣil has withdrawn from us' directly gives the sect its name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسبب تسمية المعتزلة معتزلة: أنَّ رئيسهم واصل بن عطاء اعتزل عن مجلس الحسن البصري يقرّر أنَّ مرتكب الكبيرة ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر، ويُثبت المنزلة بين المنزلتين، فقال الحسن: اعتزل عنا واصل.
And the reason for naming the Mu'tazila as Mu'tazila: that their leader Wāṣil ibn 'Aṭā' withdrew from the study circle of al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī, while he was establishing that the committer of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, and affirming the intermediate position between the two positions. So al-Ḥasan said: 'Wāṣil has withdrawn from us.'
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage provides a brief historical etymology of the name 'Mu'tazila' (the Withdrawers). It states that the sect's founder, Wāṣil ibn 'Aṭā', held a theological opinion that differed from his teacher al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī. Specifically, Wāṣil argued that a Muslim who commits a major sin (kabīra) is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but occupies an intermediate station (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn). When he presented this view in al-Ḥasan's circle, al-Ḥasan declared him to have withdrawn (iʿtazala), hence the name. The passage thus connects the origin of the sect's name to this specific doctrinal disagreement over the status of grave sinners.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
واصل بن عطاء
الحسن البصري
الكبيرة
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
اعْتَزَلَ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila were so named because they 'withdrew' from the Muslim community.
Correction: The name originated from a specific event in al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī's study circle, where Wāṣil withdrew due to a doctrinal difference, not from a general withdrawal from the community.

Checkpoint
What doctrine was Wāṣil ibn 'Aṭā' establishing that led to the naming of the Mu'tazila?
A) That the Quran is created.
B) That God is just and must do what is best.
C) That the committer of a major sin is in an intermediate position between belief and disbelief.
D) That faith increases and decreases.

Key Takeaway
The name 'Mu'tazila' derives from Wāṣil's withdrawal from al-Ḥasan's circle over the doctrine of the intermediate position for grave sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Wāṣil ibn 'Aṭā' was labeled by his teacher's statement. Have you ever been labeled because of a stance you took? In what way did that label shape how others viewed you, and how did you respond? Commit to one action you can take to ensure you don't let a label define your identity more than your faith does.
?
The Mu'tazila became known for a position they held on major sins. Think of a belief you hold that might separate you from a community or teacher. Is that belief grounded in the Quran and Sunnah, or is it a matter of personal opinion? Are you willing to be defined by it? Write a short prayer asking Allah for guidance in holding onto truth while maintaining unity.

The Origin of the Name Mu'tazila
Historical anecdote on the naming of the Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical event that gave the Mu'tazila their name and the theological issue behind it.

Core Idea
A withdrawal over a single doctrine gave a whole sect its name.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary explains a famous historical anecdote about the origin of the term 'Mu'tazila.' It shows how a theological disagreement over the status of the grave sinner led to a physical withdrawal from a teacher's circle, which became the sect's namesake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2
Identify the key elements

The doctrine 'al-manzilah bayna al-manzilatayn' is a hallmark of Mu'tazilite thought.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why did this withdrawal lead to a lasting name? What does this tell you about the early formation of theological schools?
This illustrates how personal disputes over doctrine could crystallize into sectarian identities.

The Argument from Facing and Its Refutation
The author presents the Mu'tazila's strongest rational argument against ru'ya and provides the Ash'ari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's strongest rational objection to seeing Allah (shubhat al-muqabala) and the Ash'ari response based on the nature of ru'ya as direct perception.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue vision requires facing; the Ash'ari reply that ru'ya is a unique perception not bound by physical laws.

The Mu'tazila raised several objections against the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. The strongest of these is the argument from facing (shubhat al-muqabala), which attempts to prove that if Allah were seen, He would have to occupy a direction and space, implying limitations incompatible with His transcendence. In this passage, the author presents this argument and then provides the Ash'ari response, which refutes the analogy between physical vision and the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall flow of argument and counterargument.
Notice the structure: objection first, then response.
2
Analyze the Mu'tazila reasoning
Break down the chain of necessary consequences they propose: facing implies direction/space, then physical conditions of vision, then the seen being a substance/accident, then seen whole or part leading to finitude or division.
Each consequence is presented as impossible for Allah, making the premise impossible.
3
Examine the Ash'ari answer
Identify the core of the response: ru'ya is a type of perception that Allah creates directly, without any physical intermediaries or spatial constraints.
The response also critiques the method of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid.
4
Reflect on the methodological flaw
Consider why analogizing unseen realities (akhira) to witnessed physical vision is invalid, and how the author uses the analogy of knowledge to support the point.
Knowledge of Allah is also perception without spatial limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخالف في ذلك المعتزلة، فأحالوها متمسكين بشُبَه ؛ أقواها شبهة المقابلة، وتقريرها : أنّه تعالى لو كان يُرى لكان مقابلاً للرائي ضرورةً، فيكون في جهة وحَيّز، ويلزم اتصال الأشعة من الباصرة بالمرئي والمسافة بين الرائي والمرئي بحيث لا يكون بعيداً جداً، ولا قريباً جداً، ولكان المرئى إمّا جوهراً وإما عرضاً، ولكان المرئي إما كله فيلزم التناهي والحصر، وإما بعضه فيلزم التّبعيض والتَّجزؤ، واللوازم كلّها محالة فالملزوم مثلها . وحاصل الجواب ما أشرنا له سابقاً من أنَّ الرُّؤية عبارة عن نوع : من الإدراك يخلقه الله متى شاء، ولأي شيء شاء، في أي محل شاء، فلا يلزم ما ذكر، وقياس الغائب على الشاهد فاسد، فكما أنّ العلم إدراك، وهم يعلمونه لا في مكان ولا جهة ولا محدوداً ولا محصوراً، فكذا الرّؤية نوع من أنواع الإدراك، فيدركونه كذلك، ومع ذلك هو انكشاف تام كما نصَّ عليه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في كثير من الأحاديث
And the Mu'tazila disagreed with that, so they deemed it impossible, holding on to doubts (shubah); the strongest of them is the doubt of facing (shubhat al-muqabala), and its presentation: that if He, Exalted, were seen, He would necessarily be facing the seer, thus He would be in a direction (jiha) and space (hayyiz), and would require the connection of rays from the seer to the seen and a distance between the seer and the seen such that it is neither too far nor too near, and the seen would be either a substance (jawhar) or an accident ('arad), and the seen would be either all of it, necessitating finitude and limitation, or part of it, necessitating division and fragmentation, and all the necessary consequences are impossible, so the original premise is likewise impossible. The summary of the answer is what we indicated previously, that vision (ru'ya) is an expression of a type of perception (idrak) that Allah creates whenever He wills, for whatever thing He wills, in whatever place He wills, so what was mentioned does not follow, and analogizing the unseen to the witnessed (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid) is corrupt. For just as knowledge is perception, and they know Him not in a place nor direction nor limited nor confined, so too vision is a type of perception, so they perceive Him in that manner, and that is a complete unveiling as stated by the Prophet ﷺ in many hadiths.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's strongest rational objection to the beatific vision and the Ash'ari response. The objection is called 'shubhat al-muqabala' (the doubt of facing). The Mu'tazila argue that if Allah were seen, He would have to be facing the seer, implying a direction and spatial location. This would require physical conditions like the emission of rays from the eye, a specific distance, and the seen object being either a substance or an accident. Moreover, the seer would either see all of Allah (necessitating finitude) or a part (necessitating division). Since all these consequences are impossible for Allah, the premise that He can be seen is impossible. The response begins by noting that this answer was already indicated earlier: ru'ya is a type of perception that Allah creates directly without physical intermediaries, so none of the mentioned physical conditions apply. The error is in analogizing the unseen (Allah in the afterlife) to the witnessed (physical vision). Just as humans know Allah without spatial or quantitative limitations, so too can they perceive Him (in the afterlife) without such limitations, as a complete unveiling attested by prophetic hadiths.

Key Terms
شبهة المقابلة
جهة
حيز
جوهر
عرض
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision necessarily requires physical proximity, direction, and spatial location.
Correction: Ru'ya in the afterlife is a direct perception created by Allah without any physical conditions; it is analogous to knowledge, which also occurs without spatial constraints.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's strongest rational argument against the possibility of seeing Allah called?
A) Shubhat al-jiha
B) Shubhat al-muqabala
C) Shubhat al-hayyiz
D) Shubhat al-idrak

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument from facing fails because the beatific vision is a direct perception created by Allah, not subject to physical conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you find yourself imposing human limitations on what Allah can do, because your intellect cannot conceive otherwise? What would it cost you to accept a clear hadith even when it contradicts your reasoning?
?
Do you long for the beatific vision as a direct, non-physical unveiling, or do you secretly imagine it as an earthly sight? How does that expectation shape your desire for the afterlife?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted on logical consistency and rejected ru'ya because it didn't fit their system. Have you ever rejected a sunna or a consensus because it didn't fit your own sense of what is fitting for Allah? Are you willing to surrender your logic to revelation?

The Ash'ari Response to Quran 6:103
The author presents the Mu'tazila's verse proof and the Ash'ari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's use of Quran 6:103 to deny the vision of Allah and the Ash'ari response that the verse negates encompassment, not vision.

Core Idea
'Eyesight does not perceive Him' means encompassment, not seeing.

In this passage, the author explains a key textual proof used by the Mu'tazila to argue against the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter. They cite the Quranic verse 'Eyesight does not perceive Him' (Quran 6:103), claiming it proves absolute inability to see. The Ash'ari response clarifies a crucial linguistic nuance that preserves both the praise of Allah and the possibility of vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the structure: first the Mu'tazila's claim, then the Ash'ari answer.
The passage is dense but clear.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage distinguishes between 'idrak' (perception/encompassment) and 'ru'ya' (vision). Understand how this difference resolves the objection.
This is the core of the Ash'ari response.
3
Understand the consequence
This distinction allows for the possibility of seeing Allah without encompassing Him, resolving the apparent contradiction with the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فأحالوها) : أي : قالوا بعدم جواز رؤيته في الدُّنيا والآخرة، بل قال أكثرهم : تجويزها كفر . قوله : (متمسكين بشبه) : أي : عقليَّة ونقليَّة ذكر العقلية وترك النَّقليَّة، وأقواها قوله تعالى : (لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ) (١)، وهو وارد مورد المدح، فيكون إدراكه بالبصر نقصاً وهو عليه محال. وأجيب عن ذلك بأنَّ معنى لا تدركه لا تحيط به على أنَّه قال : لا تدركه، ولم يقل : لا تراه ؛ فالأبصار لا تحيط به، كما أنَّ العقول لا تحيط به.
His saying: 'So they deemed it impossible' meaning: they said it is not permissible to see Him in this world and the Hereafter; rather most of them said: deeming it permissible is disbelief. His saying: 'holding on to doubts' meaning: rational and transmitted; he mentioned the rational and left the transmitted, and the strongest of them is His saying, Exalted: 'Eyesight does not perceive Him' (6:103), and it is presented in the context of praise, so perceiving Him with sight would be a deficiency, and that is impossible for Him. And it was answered that the meaning of 'does not perceive Him' is 'does not encompass Him'; because He said 'does not perceive' and did not say 'does not see'; so eyesight does not encompass Him, just as minds do not encompass Him.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage deals with a second objection from the Mu'tazila, this time a textual one from the Quran. The commentary explains that the Mu'tazila deemed ru'ya impossible in both worlds, with most considering belief in its possibility as disbelief. Among their doubts, the strongest transmitted one is the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (Eyesight does not perceive Him, Qur'an 6:103). They argue that this verse is in a context of praise, implying that being perceived by sight would be a deficiency, which is impossible for Allah. The Ash'ari response clarifies that the verse means 'does not encompass' (la yuhitu bihi), not 'does not see' (la yarahu). The distinction is crucial: eyesight can see without fully encompassing, just as minds know Allah without fully comprehending Him. Thus the verse does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah; it negates the possibility of fully encompassing Him with sight.

Key Terms
لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ
إدراك
إحاطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quran 6:103 proves that Allah cannot be seen at all.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows it only negates encompassment, not vision.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response in the passage, what does 'لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ' (eyesight does not perceive Him) actually mean?
A) Eyesight does not see Him at all
B) Eyesight does not encompass Him
C) Eyesight cannot reach Him
D) Eyesight is blinded by His glory

Key Takeaway
The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' negates encompassment, not vision, allowing for the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila limited Allah based on their logic. Are there areas in your life where you subtly impose human limitations on Allah—expecting Him to act 'reasonably' or 'fairly' by your standards? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to His revelation even when it defies your expectations?
?
The verse was meant as praise, yet some turned it into a denial. Reflect: In your worship, do you ever praise Allah in ways that actually restrict His greatness (e.g., assuming He cannot forgive a certain sin)? How can you praise Him with awe that leaves room for His infinite mercy and power?
?
The Ash'ari response shows that not fully comprehending Allah does not mean not seeing Him. Do you long to see Allah in the Hereafter? Or has the fear of anthropomorphism or your own unworthiness made you content with distance? What would it mean for your heart to yearn for His vision without trying to limit Him?
