(۱) ما بين معقوفين في النسخ: (وتسعين)، والصواب ما أثبت كما في الطبقات الكبرى».
(۲) الطبقات الكبرى (۱۲۸/۲۲ (۲۲۷) .
Biographies of men of Sufism. Until the leadership in the sciences of the folk and solving their problems ended with him, and many people gathered with him and had good belief in him. Ibn Khallikan and others said: 'They are the Rifa'i sect, and they are called the Ahmadis and the Bata'ihis, and they have wonderful states: eating snakes alive, descending into ovens while they are blazing with fire, entering into furnaces, and one of them sleeps on the side of the oven while the baker bakes on the other side, and they kindle a great fire, and they hold a samā' (listening session) and they dance to it until it is extinguished [؟]. And God often manifested the Truth to him with majesty, so he would melt until he became a pool of water, then mercy would reach him and he would freeze little by little until he returned to his normal body, and he would say to his group: 'Were it not for God's kindness, I would not have returned to you.' In God, the name of one of them: Ma'ali, and the other: Abd al-Mun'im, so they went out one day to the desert, and one of them wished for a document of emancipation from the Fire to descend from the sky, so a white leaf fell from it, and they saw no writing on it, so they came to the Shaykh without telling him the story, and he looked at them then fell prostrate. And he said: 'Praise be to God Who showed me the emancipation of my companions from the Fire in this world before the Hereafter.' So it was said to him: 'This is white.' He said: 'O my sons, the hand of power does not write black, and this is written with light.' (1) Wafayat al-A'yan (1/171). When he performed Hajj, he stood facing the noble Prophetic chamber and recited (al-basīt): 'I used to send her kissing the earth for me, so she is my deputy in the state of distance; my soul and this turn of bodies have come, so extend your hand that my lips may attain it.' Then the noble hand came out from the grave until he kissed it while people were looking at it. And he informed of the time of his death and its description, and it was as he said. And he wanted to buy a garden, but its owner refused to sell it except for a palace in Paradise, so he trembled, changed color, and became pale, then said: 'I have bought it from you for that.' He said: 'Write me your contract.' So he wrote: 'In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. This is what Isma'il bought from the servant Ahmad ibn al-Rifa'i, guaranteeing upon God's generosity for him a palace in Paradise, with boundaries: first: the Garden of Eden, second: the Garden of Refuge, third: the Garden of Eternity, fourth: the Garden of Paradise with all its houris, servants, furnishings, drinks, rivers, and trees in exchange for his garden in this world. And God is a witness and guarantor over that. Isma'il died; the paper was buried with him, and they awoke to find written on his grave: 'We have found what our Lord promised us to be true.'' So when he died in his town in the year 578, and he had no offspring, but the shaykhdom was for his nephew. (1) What is in brackets in the copies: (ninety), and the correct is what was established as in al-Tabaqat al-Kubra. (2) al-Tabaqat al-Kubra (22/128, 227).
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a hagiographic account of the Sufi saint Ahmad al-Rifa'i, founder of the Rifa'i order. It begins by stating his supreme authority in the sciences of Sufism. Then it cites Ibn Khallikan to describe the distinct practices of his followers, such as eating live snakes, entering blazing ovens, and dancing in fire until it is extinguished. The text then relates personal miracles: a divine manifestation that caused him to melt into water, then solidify again; a story of two men who received an invisible white leaf from heaven, which he interpreted as a document of emancipation written in light; a miracle during his pilgrimage where the Prophet's hand emerged from the grave for him to kiss; and finally, a transaction where he bought a garden in exchange for a palace in Paradise, with a written contract that was buried with the seller. The passage concludes with his death date and note that his nephew succeeded him. The footnotes refer to biographical sources that correct the death year.

The True Shaykh and the Danger of Deviant Aqidah
States criteria for a genuine shaykh and warns against those with corrupt beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criteria for a genuine spiritual guide and the danger of deviating from Sunni creed while following a madhhab.

Core Idea
Correct creed is essential; following a madhhab in fiqh is not enough.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary emphasizes that a true shaykh must have undergone spiritual training under a qualified master. It then warns that many people claim adherence to the four Sunni imams in jurisprudence, yet hold beliefs that contradict Ahl al-Sunnah. The lesson establishes the priority of sound aqidah over mere outer conformity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the two conditions
Find the condition for a true shaykh and then the warning about deviant beliefs.
Notice the shift from positive requirement to negative warning.
3
Reflect on the key message
Consider why the author stresses that following a madhhab in fiqh does not guarantee correct aqidah.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالشَّيخُ الذي يدلُّ على الله يجب أن يكون قد سلك على طريقة شيخ من مشايخ الطريق، وتعب وجاهد نفسه حتّى تهذبت وزالت عنها الرُّعونات البشريَّةُ ، وإلا فيجب اجتنابه، فإنَّ كثيراً من النَّاس من قلَّد إماماً من الأئمة الأربعة الله لهم ، ولكنَّه في عقائده زاغ عن اعتقادهم، فلم يعتقد مُعتَقَدَ أهلِ السُّنَّة، وهم فرق شتّى قد ضلُّوا في عقائدهم
So the shaykh who guides to Allah must have traversed upon the path of a shaykh from the mashayikh of the Path, and labored and struggled with his soul until it became refined and the human follies left it; otherwise, he must be avoided. For many people have followed an imam from the four imams—Allah [be with them]—but in their beliefs they deviated from their belief, so they did not believe the belief of Ahl al-Sunnah, and they are various sects who have gone astray in their beliefs.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement about the necessary conditions for a true spiritual guide (shaykh) and a warning against those who claim guidance while holding corrupt beliefs. It opens with the requirement that the shaykh himself must have traveled the Sufi path under a qualified master, exerted effort in disciplining his soul until it is purified and free from human frivolity. If a shaykh lacks this, he must be shunned. Then it shifts to a broader warning: many people outwardly follow one of the four Sunni imams in jurisprudence, but in their creed (aqidah) they deviate from the beliefs of those imams. They do not adhere to the creed of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and they belong to various misguided sects. The passage thus emphasizes that correct 'aqidah is essential; mere adherence in fiqh is insufficient, and one must avoid those whose beliefs stray from Sunni orthodoxy, even if they claim to follow a Sunni madhhab.

أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Following a madhhab in fiqh ensures correct beliefs about Allah.
Correction: The passage warns that many who follow an imam in fiqh deviate in aqidah, so one must also verify creedal orthodoxy.

Checkpoint

D) He must have been born into a family of scholars.

Key Takeaway
A true spiritual guide must have undergone rigorous self-discipline, and following a madhhab in jurisprudence does not guarantee sound creed; correct aqidah is paramount.

Two Categories of People Rejecting Sunni Orthodoxy
Al-Sawi classifies two misguided groups beyond those who follow an imam with deviation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two groups described by al-Sawi as rejecting Sunni orthodoxy and understand why the first group is considered more astray.

Core Idea
Rejecting both taqlid of the four imams and Sunni creed leads to greater misguidance.

In this passage, al-Sawi distinguishes between those who outright reject all Sunni authorities and those who falsely claim to follow the spiritual path. This classification helps clarify the boundaries of authentic Sunni Islam and warns against two forms of deviation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise style and the comparative judgment ('more astray').
The passage is brief but packs two distinct groups.
2
Identify the two groups
The first group rejects both taqlid of the four imams and the creed of Ahl al-Sunna. The second group claims to be traversing the path of Allah's people (Sufi path).
The first group is judged 'more astray' than a previously mentioned group.
3
Understand the author's judgment
Al-Sawi says the first group is 'more astray than those before them' – meaning those who follow an imam but have some deviation. Rejecting both authority and creed is worse.
This shows a hierarchy of misguidance.
4
Reflect on the second group
The second group makes a claim without proper adherence. The implication is that true spiritual following requires submission to Sunni orthodoxy and a qualified guide.
The passage warns against false claims to spirituality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن النَّاس من لم يرضَ بتقليد إمام من الأئمة الأربعة، ولا باعتقاد أهل السُّنَّة، وهم أضلُّ ممَّن قبلهم. ومن النَّاس من يزعم أنَّه سالك طريق أهل الله تعالى.
And among the people are those who are not satisfied with taqlid of an imam from the four imams, nor with the belief of Ahl al-Sunna, and they are more astray than those before them. And among the people are those who claim that they are traversing the path of the people of God most high.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a brief classification of two more misguided groups. The first group consists of those who reject both following (taqlid) of the four Sunni imams and the creed of Ahl al-Sunna. The author declares them more astray than the previously mentioned group (those who follow an imam but deviate in creed). The second group is those who claim to be on the Sufi path but presumably are not genuinely following the qualified shaykh as described in the earlier biography. The passage implies that such claims are false without proper adherence to Sunni orthodoxy and spiritual discipline.

Misconception: Some think that rejecting taqlid entirely is commendable or that following Sufi path alone suffices without adherence to the four imams and Sunni creed.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that rejecting both taqlid and Sunni creed is more astray even than following an imam with deviation. True spirituality is built on Sunni orthodoxy.

Key Takeaway
To reject both the four imams' guidance and Sunni creed is to be more astray even than those who follow an imam with some deviation.

The Qadariyya and Other Deviant Sects
Defines two Qadariyya sects and lists other deviant sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two sects of Qadariyya and their rulings, and be introduced to other deviant sects mentioned in the hadith of 73 sects.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya are not one but two sects, with different errors and rulings.

This passage defines the Qadariyya and lists other sects mentioned in the famous hadith of 73 sects. Understanding these classifications helps one recognize theological errors and the boundaries of orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a general statement about sects.
2
Identify the two Qadariyya sects
Find the two sects described. Note what each denies or affirms about God's knowledge and human actions, and the ruling given for each.
The first sect denies prior knowledge; the second affirms it but errs on actions.
3
Note the other sects listed
The passage then lists three other groups: philosophers, Sumaniyya, and Mujassima. These are among the 72 sects that the Prophet said would be in Hell.
These are just examples; there are many more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق كالقدرية وغيرهم. قوله : ( كالقدرية : هم فرقتان؛ الأولى : تنكر تعلق علم الله بالأشياء قبل وجودها، وتقول : إنَّما يعلمها حال وقوعها، وهذه الفرقة انقرضت قبل ظهور الإمام الشافعي، وفرقة ثانية تقول: الله يعلم الأشياء قبل وجودها أنَّ أفعال العباد مقدورة لهم، وواقعة منهم استقلالاً؛ بسبب إقدار الله لهم، والأولى : كفَّار، والثانية : فسَّاق . قوله : (وغيرهم) : أي : كالفلاسفة والسُّمنية والمجسمة وباقي الفرق الاثنين وسبعين .
The sects are like the Qadariyya and others. He said: (Like the Qadariyya)—they are two sects: the first denies the attachment of God's knowledge to things before their occurrence, and says He knows them only at the time they occur; this sect became extinct before the emergence of Imam al-Shafi'i. The second sect says: God knows things before their existence, but that the actions of servants are within their power and occur from them independently, due to God's enabling them. The first are disbelievers, and the second are sinners. He said: (And others)—meaning: like the philosophers, the Sumaniyya, the Mujassima, and the rest of the seventy-two sects.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of deviant sects, specifically commenting on the phrase 'the sects are like the Qadariyya and others' from the original text. It first defines the Qadariyya, dividing them into two subgroups. The first subgroup denies that God knows things before they happen, asserting that He only knows them at the moment they occur. This group died out before Imam al-Shafi'i. The second subgroup affirms God's foreknowledge but claims that human actions are independently within human power and occur from them autonomously, even though God gives them the ability. The ruling: the first group are disbelievers (kuffar), the second are sinners (fussaq). The passage then explains 'others' by listing three groups: philosophers (falasifa), Sumaniyya (a sect that denies prophethood or believes in reincarnation), and Mujassima (anthropomorphists who liken God to bodies), and states that these are among the 72 sects that the Prophet said would be in Hell, following the hadith that the Muslim community will split into 73 sects.

Key Terms
قدرية
علم الله
كفار
فساق
فلاسفة
سمنية
مجسمة
72 فرقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qadariyya are a single sect that denies predestination entirely.
Correction: The Qadariyya actually have two sects: one extreme (denying prior knowledge) and one moderate (affirming knowledge but claiming independent action). The first became extinct.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for the first Qadariyya sect?
A) They are sinners (fussaq).
B) They are disbelievers (kuffar).
C) They are misguided but not disbelievers.
D) They are considered Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya are divided into two distinct sects with different errors and rulings, and they are part of the 72 deviant sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah knows every action you will ever take before you do it, even the sinful ones? If so, how does that knowledge change the way you approach repentance and self-improvement?
?
The second Qadariyya sect believed that while Allah knows all, humans act independently. Can you identify any area in your life where you subtly attribute your successes or failures solely to your own ability, forgetting that Allah is the Creator of your actions?
?
If you were to meet someone today who unwittingly holds the belief of the second Qadariyya—thinking they have full independence in their actions—what would you say to them, and how would you back it up with the very words of this lesson?

The Two Qadariyya Subsects and Other Deviant Sects
The commentator defines the two Qadariyya subsects and lists other deviant sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two subsects of the Qadariyya, their beliefs regarding divine knowledge and human action, and how they differ from other deviant sects.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya are not all alike: one subsect denies Allah’s foreknowledge, the other affirms it but claims independent human action.

The commentator begins by unpacking the phrase 'al-firaq ka-al-Qadariyya wa-ghayrihim' (the sects, like the Qadariyya and others). He provides a crucial distinction within the Qadariyya, showing that they are not a monolithic group. After detailing their beliefs and rulings, he briefly lists other deviant sects. This lesson establishes a key taxonomy of heretical groups.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the commentator first glosses the term 'Qadariyya' and then divides them into two groups.
Pay attention to the ruling (kuffār vs. fussāq) for each group.
2
Understand the first Qadariyya subsect
Identify what the first group believes about Allah's knowledge. They deny that Allah knows things before they exist. Note that they are called disbelievers (kuffār) and that they became extinct before Imam al-Shafi'i.
3
Understand the second Qadariyya subsect
The second group affirms Allah's foreknowledge but claims that human actions are independently performed by the servants. They are considered sinful (fussāq) but not disbelievers.
4
Learn about the other deviant sects
The commentator mentions 'ghayrihim' including philosophers, Samaniyya, Mujassima, and the remaining seventy-two sects. Note that these are all considered deviant.
The number 72 comes from the hadith about the division of the Ummah.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفِرَق كالقدرية وغيرهم.
قوله : (كالقدرية : هم فرقتان؛ الأولى : تنكر تعلق علم الله بالأشياء قبل وجودها، وتقول : إنَّما يعلمها حال وقوعها، وهذه الفرقة انقرضت قبل ظهور الإمام الشافعي، وفرقة ثانية تقول: الله يعلم الأشياء قبل وجودها أنَّ أفعال العباد مقدورة لهم، وواقعة منهم استقلالاً؛ بسبب إقدار الله لهم، والأولى : كفَّار، والثانية : فسَّاق .
قوله : (وغيرهم) : أي : كالفلاسفة والسُّمنية والمجسمة وباقي الفرق الاثنين وسبعين .
The sects: like the Qadariyya and others. He said: (Like the Qadariyya: they are two groups; the first: denies the attachment of Allah's knowledge to things before their existence, and says: He only knows them at the time of their occurrence, and this group became extinct before the appearance of Imam al-Shafi'i. A second group says: Allah knows things before their existence, but the actions of servants are within their power, occurring from them independently, due to Allah enabling them. The first: are disbelievers (kuffār), and the second: are sinful (fussāq). He said: (And others): i.e., like the philosophers, the Samaniyya, the Mujassima (anthropomorphists), and the remaining seventy-two sects.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a classified enumeration of deviant sects, focusing first on the Qadariyya. The commentator begins by citing the matn phrase 'Al-Firaq ka-al-Qadariyya wa-ghayrihim' (The sects: like the Qadariyya and others). He then explains the Qadariyya in detail, dividing them into two subsects. The first subsect denies that Allah's knowledge encompasses things before they exist, claiming He only knows them when they happen. This group, the commentator notes, died out before Imam al-Shafi'i. The second subsect affirms Allah's foreknowledge but insists that human actions are independently produced by the servants themselves, though with Allah's enabling power (iqdār). The commentator then gives the ruling for each: the first subsect are disbelievers (kuffār), the second are sinners (fussāq). After this, he glosses 'wa-ghayrihim' (and others) by listing additional deviant groups: the philosophers (falāsifa), the Samaniyya (a sect denying prophecy), the Mujassima (anthropomorphists), and all the other seventy-two sects mentioned in hadith. This lesson establishes a key taxonomy of heretical groups, distinguishing between those who deny divine attributes and those who err in predestination.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Qadariyya are the same in belief and ruling.
Correction: The passage shows two distinct subsects: one denies Allah's foreknowledge (disbelievers), the other affirms it but claims independent action (sinners).

Checkpoint

A) The first denies qadar entirely, the second affirms it.
B) The first denies Allah's foreknowledge of things before they exist, the second affirms it but claims human acts are independent.
C) The first believes in free will, the second in predestination.
D) The first are disbelievers, the second are Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya are divided into two groups: one denies Allah's foreknowledge (kuffār), the other affirms it but claims independent human action (fussāq), and many other sects also deviate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Examine your daily choices—do you truly submit to Allah’s will, or do you live as if your actions are solely your own? How will you change this today?
?
The first subsect became extinct, yet their denial of Allah’s foreknowledge still echoes in modern doubts. When you face uncertainty about the future, do you trust that Allah already knows and has decreed it? What are you holding onto that reveals a hidden belief in your own independence from His knowledge?
?
The commentator labels the first group as disbelievers and the second as sinners. Do you take these rulings lightly? Which of your own beliefs or actions might place you closer to these deviant views? What concrete step will you take to align your heart with Ahl al-Sunnah's understanding of qadar?

Exposing False Ascetics
The commentator describes false Sufis and explains a poetic critique by Ibn al-Farid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator exposes false ascetics who use outward piety to mask their worldly greed, and how Ibn al-Farid's poetry reinforces this critique.

Core Idea
Outward piety can be a mask for worldly greed; true spirituality is not feigned.

This passage addresses a recurring problem in spiritual communities: those who adopt the external signs of piety—coarse garments, Sufi jargon—but whose inner lives are consumed by worldly desires. The commentator, supported by a verse from the celebrated mystic poet Ibn al-Farid, pulls back the curtain on such impostors. As you read, notice how the description moves from external appearance to inner reality, and how the poetry serves as a divine witness against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's description
Start with the commentator's vivid description of the false ascetic: his appearance, speech, and hidden behavior. Identify the key actions (eating, sleeping, pouncing on the world) and the consequences (deceiving people, gaining followers).
Pay attention to the metaphor of the beast pouncing on prey.
2
Analyze Ibn al-Farid's poetry
Read the two lines of poetry. Notice how they contrast claims of love (da'wa) with actual spiritual progress. The phrase 'they did not move from their place' is particularly powerful.
The poetry is from a longer ode; here it serves as a direct indictment.
3
Connect poetry to commentary
The commentator explains the poetry's references: 'content with wishes' means they aspire only to worldly shaykhhood. See how the poetry reinforces the critique of hypocrisy.
The commentary on 'kullu' (they became weary) shows their effort was wasted.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Ask yourself: What distinguishes a true seeker from a false one? The passage emphasizes that external forms can be deceptive; the real test is one's relationship with the world and sincerity in seeking God.
This connects to broader themes of purification of intention (ikhlas).

He put on their appearance, and speaks in a way that makes people think he is one of them, but the reality is that he is idle, filling his belly with food, whether lawful or unlawful, and his night is spent sleeping, and he pounces upon the world like the pounce of a beast upon prey, and perhaps he makes himself a shaykh, with followers who hunt for him, through the snare of his shaykhhood, the filth of perishable goods, and they claim to be upon something. Those are the liars. Indeed, the knower of Allah, my master Umar ibn al-Farid, pointed to them in his saying (1): [from al-Tawil]
They were content with wishes and tested by their fortunes, and they plunged into the seas of love as a claim, but they were not tested.
So they, in the night journey, did not move from their place, and they did not travel in the journey away from Him, though they became weary.
He said: (He put on their appearance) i.e., wearing coarse garments and the like.
He said: (And he pounces upon the world) i.e., he hastens and falls upon acquiring it.
He said: (They were content with wishes): the pronoun refers to the people explicitly mentioned in his saying (1): 'A group presented themselves for love and turned away with their side from its soundness and became ill.' And what is meant by wishes is what they wished for themselves, and they stopped at it, which is presenting for shaykhhood for the sake of acquiring the world.
He said: (And they became weary) i.e., they got tired and did not attain anything.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and exposure of false ascetics who claim Sufism but are worldly. The author begins by describing a person who adopts the external appearance of the righteous (wearing coarse garments) and speaks in a way that deludes people into thinking he is among them, yet in reality he is lazy, gluttonous (eating regardless of legality), sleeps all night, and greedily seizes worldly goods like a beast pouncing on prey. He may even set himself up as a shaykh with followers who exploit his pretence to gather worldly detritus, while claiming to be on something meaningful. The author declares these are the liars. He then cites Ibn al-Farid's poetry as a witness: these people indulge in wishful thinking (amānī) and are content with their worldly lot, but they dive into the sea of love only as a claim (daʿwā) and are not truly tested; they remain stationary in their spiritual journey, not moving from their place, and wear themselves out without attaining anything. The commentary then explains key phrases: 'fayatazayyā bi-zayyihim' means wearing coarse clothes; 'yathibu ʿalā al-dunyā' means rushing to acquire the world; the pronoun in 'raḍū bi-al-amānī' refers to the same group who present themselves for love but turn away from its truth, and the wishes are their aspiration for shaykhhood to gain worldly benefits; 'qad kullū' means they tired without obtaining anything. This lesson exposes the hypocrisy of false spirituality and emphasises that true spiritual station is not achieved by mere pretense.

الأماني

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Outer piety (e.g., coarse garments, speaking about spiritual matters) is sufficient to be considered a true ascetic or Sufi.
Correction: The passage shows that such outward signs can be a deceptive mask; genuine spirituality is proven by one's inner state and actions, particularly detachment from worldly greed and sincerity in seeking God.

A) He is deeply spiritual and satisfied.
B) He is idle, gluttonous, and greedy for the world.
C) He is a sincere shaykh with many followers.
D) He travels extensively in search of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
True spirituality is not demonstrated by outward appearance or claims, but by sincere detachment from worldly greed and actual progress on the spiritual path.

The Deceptive Ascetic: Outward Garb, Hidden Worldliness
Critique of false ascetics and their worldly motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the traits of false spiritual claimants who adopt outward asceticism while pursuing worldly gains, as critiqued by al-Sawi.

Core Idea
Outward piety without inner sincerity is a trap for worldly gain.

Al-Sawi begins by exposing a common hypocrisy: those who dress and speak like the righteous but whose hearts are attached to the world. This lesson examines his sharp critique, supported by a poem from Ibn al-Farid, to help you distinguish genuine spirituality from mere posturing.

Guided Path

3

The commentary explains 'wishes' as what they wish for themselves, stopping at the exposure for shaykhdom for worldly gain.
4

This prepares you for the next lesson on the true path.

He adorns himself in their garb, and speaks in a way that makes people think he is one of them, while in reality he is a idle person, filling his belly with food, whether lawful or unlawful, and his night with sleep, and he pounces on the world like a beast pouncing on its prey. Those are the liars. The knower of Allah Most High, my master Umar ibn al-Farid, may Allah be pleased with him, has pointed to them in his saying: [from the long meter] 'They were satisfied with wishes and were tested by their shares, and they plunged into the seas of love by claim, so they were not tested. They remained in the journey, not moving from their place, and they did not travel in the path away from it, and they became exhausted.' His saying: 'He adorns himself in their garb' meaning he wears coarse clothing and the like. His saying: 'and he pounces on the world' meaning he hastens and applies himself to acquiring it. His saying: 'They were satisfied with wishes' - the pronoun refers to the people mentioned in the line 'A people exposed themselves to love and turned away...' The meaning of 'wishes' is what they wished for themselves, and they stopped at it - that is, exposing themselves to shaykhdom in order to acquire the world. The principles of the path that leads to Allah. Rather, they fell behind and retreated backwards because they followed their own whims, and Satan leads them to everything he loves, as he said: [from the long meter] 'And from my doctrine, when they preferred blindness over guidance, out of envy from themselves, they went astray.' Until it became part of their character that if someone gives them charity or honors them with a kindness, they take that as a custom, and they demand it from whoever does good to them, so that they make the paths narrow for him, and they say: 'Give us our custom, otherwise we will discredit you.' So they give people the impression that they are masters of states, and that Allah Most High verifies them in their words. No! That is not the way of the poor (fuqara'), the people of Allah. Their way is humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, preference, and trust. As for these, they are the worst of people, they consume people's wealth unjustly, and they claim high stations while they are in the lowest depths, and they have become numerous in this time. His saying: 'And from my doctrine' is connected to his saying 'they went astray.' His saying: 'When they preferred' meaning when they loved the transient and preferred it over the everlasting, which is blindness. His saying: 'over guidance' meaning instead of it. His saying: 'out of envy' is a causative object; meaning they loved the immediate delights instead of guidance because of their envy of the people of the path regarding their states and stations, so they adorned themselves in their garb in form, but did not do like their actions. (1) Diwan of Ibn al-Farid, p.135.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a critique of false claimants to Sufism, followed by a description of the true path of the spiritual poor (fuqara'). The text begins by describing a hypocritical individual who outwardly adopts the dress and speech of the righteous to deceive people, but in reality is a glutton for worldly gains, pouncing on the world like a beast. He appoints himself as a shaykh and has followers who help him collect worldly debris through the trap of his pretended spiritual authority. These are declared liars. Then a poem by Ibn al-Farid is cited, describing such people as content with mere wishes but never truly embarking on the spiritual journey. The commentary explains each phrase: 'adorns himself in their garb' refers to wearing rough clothes, 'pounces on the world' indicates speed in seeking wealth, and the wishes are for becoming a shaykh for worldly gain. The second part, on page 466, discusses the true foundations of the path to Allah. It states that these false ones have regressed because they followed their own desires and are led by Satan. A second poem describes their envy leading them to prefer blindness (worldly desires) over guidance. Their behavior goes so far as to demand gifts and favors as a customary right, threatening to discredit those who do not comply. They pretend to have spiritual states, but the author firmly denies that this is the way of the true poor (fuqara'). The true path is characterized by humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, preference of others, and trust in Allah. In contrast, these false individuals are the worst of people, consuming wealth unjustly, claiming high ranks while being in low depths, and are prevalent in modern times. The commentary clarifies the poetic verses: 'from my doctrine' connects to 'they went astray'; 'preferred blindness' means preferring the transient over the eternal; 'over guidance' means instead of it; and 'out of envy' explains that they loved immediate pleasures because of envy of the true spiritual people, imitating only their external appearance without genuine action.

حظوظ (worldly shares)

Key Takeaway
Outward piety without inner sincerity is a deception that ultimately serves worldly desires, not the path to Allah.

?

?

?
What specific area of your religious life is most vulnerable to becoming a cover for worldly attachments? What would it cost you to strip away that cover and face yourself honestly before Allah?

The True Path: Humility and Trust in Allah
Description of the true path of humility and trust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true characteristics of the spiritual poor (fuqara') as humility, trust, and other virtues, contrasting with the false claimants.

Core Idea
True spirituality is marked by humility, not demand for gifts.

Having exposed the false, al-Sawi now lays out the authentic path of the people of Allah. This lesson explains the core virtues that define a true seeker, and contrasts them with the behavior of those who claim high stations while being in low depths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic from 'أصول الطريق' to the end, then the translation. Notice the shift from critique to positive description.
Pay attention to the list of virtues: humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, preference, trust.
2
Analyze the second poem
The poem says they preferred blindness over guidance out of envy. Identify what 'blindness' represents: following whims and worldly desires.
The commentary clarifies that 'blindness' is the transient world, and they envied the true people's states.
3
Contrast false and true
List the behaviors of false claimants (demanding gifts, threatening discredit) vs. the true way (humility, trust). Notice how each virtue opposes a vice.
4
Reflect on application
Consider how these virtues can be practiced today. The true path is about inner transformation, not external demands or appearances.
Al-Sawi warns that such false people are numerous in his time, so discernment is needed.

He adorns himself in their garb, and speaks in a way that makes people think he is one of them, while in reality he is a idle person, filling his belly with food, whether lawful or unlawful, and his night with sleep, and he pounces on the world like a beast pouncing on its prey. Perhaps he makes himself a shaykh, and he has followers who hunt for him, by means of the net of his shaykhdom, the garbage of the perishing debris, and they claim that they are upon something. Those are the liars. The knower of Allah Most High, my master Umar ibn al-Farid, may Allah be pleased with him, has pointed to them in his saying: [from the long meter] 'They were satisfied with wishes and were tested by their shares, and they plunged into the seas of love by claim, so they were not tested. They remained in the journey, not moving from their place, and they did not travel in the path away from it, and they became exhausted.' His saying: 'He adorns himself in their garb' meaning he wears coarse clothing and the like. His saying: 'and he pounces on the world' meaning he hastens and applies himself to acquiring it. His saying: 'They were satisfied with wishes' - the pronoun refers to the people mentioned in the line 'A people exposed themselves to love and turned away...' The meaning of 'wishes' is what they wished for themselves, and they stopped at it - that is, exposing themselves to shaykhdom in order to acquire the world. His saying: 'and they became exhausted' meaning they tired and did not attain anything. (1) Diwan of Ibn al-Farid, p.134. (2) The line is from Ibn al-Farid in his Diwan, p.134. The principles of the path that leads to Allah. Rather, they fell behind and retreated backwards because they followed their own whims, and Satan leads them to everything he loves, as he said: [from the long meter] 'And from my doctrine, when they preferred blindness over guidance, out of envy from themselves, they went astray.' Until it became part of their character that if someone gives them charity or honors them with a kindness, they take that as a custom, and they demand it from whoever does good to them, so that they make the paths narrow for him, and they say: 'Give us our custom, otherwise we will discredit you.' So they give people the impression that they are masters of states, and that Allah Most High verifies them in their words. No! That is not the way of the poor (fuqara'), the people of Allah. Their way is humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, preference, and trust. As for these, they are the worst of people, they consume people's wealth unjustly, and they claim high stations while they are in the lowest depths, and they have become numerous in this time. His saying: 'And from my doctrine' is connected to his saying 'they went astray.' His saying: 'When they preferred' meaning when they loved the transient and preferred it over the everlasting, which is blindness. His saying: 'over guidance' meaning instead of it. His saying: 'out of envy' is a causative object; meaning they loved the immediate delights instead of guidance because of their envy of the people of the path regarding their states and stations, so they adorned themselves in their garb in form, but did not do like their actions. (1) Diwan of Ibn al-Farid, p.135.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a critique of false claimants to Sufism, followed by a description of the true path of the spiritual poor (fuqara'). The text begins by describing a hypocritical individual who outwardly adopts the dress and speech of the righteous to deceive people, but in reality is a glutton for worldly gains, pouncing on the world like a beast. He appoints himself as a shaykh and has followers who help him collect worldly debris through the trap of his pretended spiritual authority. These are declared liars. Then a poem by Ibn al-Farid is cited, describing such people as content with mere wishes but never truly embarking on the spiritual journey. The commentary explains each phrase: 'adorns himself in their garb' refers to wearing rough clothes, 'pounces on the world' indicates speed in seeking wealth, and the wishes are for becoming a shaykh for worldly gain. The second part, on page 466, discusses the true foundations of the path to Allah. It states that these false ones have regressed because they followed their own desires and are led by Satan. A second poem describes their envy leading them to prefer blindness (worldly desires) over guidance. Their behavior goes so far as to demand gifts and favors as a customary right, threatening to discredit those who do not comply. They pretend to have spiritual states, but the author firmly denies that this is the way of the true poor (fuqara'). The true path is characterized by humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, preference of others, and trust in Allah. In contrast, these false individuals are the worst of people, consuming wealth unjustly, claiming high ranks while being in low depths, and are prevalent in modern times. The commentary clarifies the poetic verses: 'from my doctrine' connects to 'they went astray'; 'preferred blindness' means preferring the transient over the eternal; 'over guidance' means instead of it; and 'out of envy' explains that they loved immediate pleasures because of envy of the true spiritual people, imitating only their external appearance without genuine action.

Key Terms
فقراء (poor)
تواضع (humility)
خمول (obscurity)
عفة (chastity)
زهد (asceticism)
ورع (scrupulousness)
إيثار (preference)
توكُل (trust)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that true spiritual guides demand gifts and favors as a right.
Correction: The passage explicitly denies this, stating that the true way is humility and trust, not demanding customs or threatening discredit.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the way of the true poor (fuqara')?
A) Demanding gifts and threatening discredit
B) Humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, preference, and trust
C) Adorning themselves in coarse clothing and claiming high stations
D) Following their whims and being led by Satan

Key Takeaway
The true path to Allah is not about outward show or demanding rights, but about cultivating humility, trust, and other inner virtues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even silently in your heart, demanded recognition or a return for a good deed you did? How does the passage's description of the true path as 'love of obscurity' challenge that tendency? What is one hidden act of charity you can do this week without telling anyone?
?
When you see someone who appears spiritually advanced, do you feel a pang of envy or a desire to imitate their outward show? The passage says the false ones went astray because of envy. Look honestly at your own heart: is there any envy that drives you to value appearances over reality? What concrete step can you take today to cultivate genuine humility instead?
?
The true path includes 'trust in Allah' as a foundation. In which area of your life are you still relying on your own efforts or expecting from people, rather than trusting Allah completely? How can you surrender that to Allah right now, and what would that look like practically tomorrow?

The Deception of False Spiritual Claimants
Al-Sawi enumerates the vices of false ascetics and contrasts them with true spiritual virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the behaviors that characterize false ascetics and why they are condemned in Islamic spirituality.

Core Idea
True spirituality is humility; false claimants demand honor and consume wealth unjustly.

Al-Sawi begins by sharply criticizing those who pretend to be on the spiritual path but in reality follow their whims and Satan. He quotes a poem by Ibn al-Farid that highlights their envy and preference for worldly gain over guidance. Then he describes their offensive behavior: they demand charity and honor as if it is their right, and they threaten to slander those who do not comply. They give the impression that they are people of spiritual states, but their actions reveal the opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the contrast between the false claimants and the true fuqara.
The passage includes a poem quotation – note how it supports the critique.
2
Identify the false behaviors
List the specific misdeeds mentioned: following whims, envy, demanding honor, threatening, and claiming lofty ranks while being low.
These are concrete signs of spiritual fraud.
3
Notice the contrast with true traits
Al-Sawi contrasts the false claimants with the 'people of Allah' (ahl Allah). Identify the eight virtues listed: humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, etc.
This contrast defines authentic spirituality.
4
Reflect on the final warning
The passage ends with a warning that such people have become numerous. Ask yourself: Why is this critique still relevant today?
This connects the lesson to lived experience.

The fundamentals of the path leading to Allah: rather they fell behind and retreated because they followed their own whims, and Satan leads them to everything he loves, as he said (1): [from the tawil meter] And concerning my sect, when they preferred blindness over guidance out of envy from themselves, they went astray. (1) [Diwan Ibn al-Farid, p. 135] Give them charity or honor them until it became part of their character that whoever gives them honor they take that as a custom, and they demand it from whoever does them a favor until they narrow the paths upon him, and they say: 'Give us our custom otherwise we will cast suspicion on you,' thus they give people the impression that they are people of states, and that Allah Almighty affirms their words. Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a poem by Ibn al-Farid, where al-Sawi explains the meaning and draws a contrast between true and false spiritual seekers. The author begins by stating that the fundamentals of the path to Allah are not what these people practice—rather, they have regressed because they followed their whims and Satan. He then quotes the poem: the poet says that concerning his own way, when people preferred spiritual blindness over guidance out of envy, they went astray. After the citation, al-Sawi elaborates that such individuals demand honor and charity as if it were their right, and they threaten to expose others if not given. They pretend to be people of spiritual states, but their real behavior is corrupt. The true way of the poor (fuqara) is exactly the opposite: humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, altruism, and trust in Allah. These false ones are the worst of people, consuming wealth falsely, claiming high ranks while being in low depths, and they have multiplied in this age. Then al-Sawi gives a word-by-word commentary of the poem line: 'concerning my sect' is connected to 'they went astray'; 'when they preferred' means they loved the transient over the eternal; 'over guidance' means instead of it; 'out of envy' is for the purpose of showing their envy of the true people of the path, so they imitated their outward appearance without doing their inner work. This lesson is a sharp critique of hypocrisy in spiritual claims.

Key Terms
التصوف
الفقراء
الزهد
الورع
الإيثار
التوكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That demanding honor and charity is a sign of spiritual status.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that such demanding behavior is the opposite of true spirituality, which is characterized by humility and love of obscurity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, what is the true way of the fuqara (the poor, spiritual seekers)?
A) Demanding honor and charity as a right
B) Humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, altruism, and trust in Allah
C) Claiming lofty spiritual ranks and receiving gifts
D) Envying others and threatening to expose them

Key Takeaway
False spiritual claimants expose themselves through demanding behavior, while true seekers embody humility, obscurity, and trust in Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt entitled to honor or recognition for your religious practice? In that moment, were you closer to the false claimants or to the people of Allah? Commit today to seek obscurity and humility in one specific act of worship this week.
?
Al-Sawi says false claimants 'consume people's wealth unjustly.' Examine your own financial dealings: do you take from others without giving full value? What would it look like to be scrupulous (wara') in your next transaction, even if it costs you personally?
?
The true path includes 'love of obscurity' — yet you likely crave being seen and praised for your good deeds. What is one hidden act of charity or service you can perform before tonight, known only to Allah, to break the hold of showing off in your heart?

Unpacking the Poem: Envy as the Root of Spiritual Fraud
Al-Sawi provides a grammatical and thematic tafsir of Ibn al-Farid's verse, focusing on envy as the driving force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and thematic explanation of Ibn al-Farid's line, particularly the role of envy in leading people astray.

Core Idea
Envy of true spiritual masters leads some to imitate their outward appearance without inner transformation.

After the general critique, al-Sawi provides a detailed word-by-word commentary on the line of poetry he quoted. This deep dive explains how each phrase connects grammatically and what it means conceptually. The key point is that the people referred to in the poem preferred immediate worldly pleasures (blindness) over guidance due to their envy of the true people of the path. They copied their external appearance but did not perform their inner work.

Guided Path
1
Read the word-by-word explanation
Read the Arabic and translation of the commentary carefully. Notice how al-Sawi links each phrase to the rest of the sentence.
The explanation is technical – focus on the meaning of each key term.
2
Identify the grammatical links
Al-Sawi clarifies that 'concerning my sect' is connected to 'they went astray' and that 'when they preferred' means 'they loved the transient'. Understand these connections.
Grammar here serves to reinforce the meaning.
3
Grasp the root cause: envy
The passage emphasizes that the motivation for choosing blindness over guidance is envy. Note how envy operates: they envy the states and ranks of the true people of the path.
Envy leads them to imitate outwardly but not truly change.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The final phrase 'They adorned themselves with their appearance outwardly, but did not work like their work' sums up the tragedy. What is the lesson for sincere seekers?
This distinction between form and substance is crucial.

This passage is a commentary on a poem by Ibn al-Farid, where al-Sawi explains the meaning and draws a contrast between true and false spiritual seekers. The author begins by stating that the fundamentals of the path to Allah are not what these people practice—rather, they have regressed because they followed their whims and Satan. He then quotes the poem: the poet says that concerning his own way, when people preferred spiritual blindness over guidance out of envy, they went astray. After the citation, al-Sawi elaborates that such individuals demand honor and charity as if it were their right, and they threaten to expose others if not given. They pretend to be people of spiritual states, but their real behavior is corrupt. The true way of the poor (fuqara) is exactly the opposite: humility, brokenness, love of obscurity, chastity, asceticism, scrupulousness, altruism, and trust in Allah. These false ones are the worst of people, consuming wealth falsely, claiming high ranks while being in low depths, and they have multiplied in this age. Then al-Sawi gives a word-by-word commentary of the poem line: 'concerning my sect' is connected to 'they went astray'; 'when they preferred' means they loved the transient over the eternal; 'over guidance' means instead of it; 'out of envy' is for the purpose of showing their envy of the true people of the path, so they imitated their outward appearance without doing their inner work. This lesson is a sharp critique of hypocrisy in spiritual claims.

Key Terms
التصوف
حسد
العمى
الهدى
الحظوظ العاجلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That outward imitation of ascetics can achieve the same spiritual rank as genuine inner transformation.
Correction: Al-Sawi explicitly says they only adorned themselves outwardly but did not work like the true people of the path, so their imitation is hollow.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, why did the people prefer blindness over guidance?
A) Because they were ignorant
B) Because they loved the eternal
C) Because of their envy of the people of the path
D) Because they were forced by Satan

Key Takeaway
Envy can lead people to imitate the external forms of spirituality while missing the inner transformation required for true closeness to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever envied someone's spiritual station so much that you tried to copy their outward actions without addressing your own inner state? What would it take for you to stop imitating and start the hard work of inner transformation today?
?
Where in your life right now are you choosing the transient—a quick comfort, a status, a distraction—over the eternal? What envy might be driving that choice, and what will you do to turn back to guidance?
?
If someone watched your daily worship and dealings, would they see a person whose outer form matches their inner reality, or someone faking it? What is one concrete step you can take this week to align your appearance with your heart's true condition before Allah?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
5 min
The Benefit of Supplication and Refutation of Mu'tazila
The commentator states the benefit of supplication and refutes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theologians affirm the benefit of supplication despite predestination, and how they refute the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
Supplication is a divinely ordained cause that brings benefit; it does not contradict divine decree.

The commentator explains a phrase from the matn that implies the benefit of du'a. He then contrasts the Sunni position with the Mu'tazila denial. This lesson will unpack that argument, showing how the Quran and Sunnah support the efficacy of prayer, and why the Mu'tazila rationalist objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation carefully. Notice the flow from explaining 'blindness' to introducing the Mu'tazila disagreement.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find where the commentator states the position of Ahl al-Haqq and where he mentions the Mu'tazila. Note the evidence each side uses.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila reasoning
The Mu'tazila argue that since Allah's decree is fixed, supplication is unnecessary. Ask yourself: does this contradict the Sunni view that du'a is beneficial? The commentator's implicit answer is no, because du'a is itself part of the decree.
The passage cuts off, but the commentary clarifies the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (المزيل للعمى) يعني : الجهل، وفي كلامه إشارة إلى أنَّ الدُّعاء ينفع، وهو ممَّا لا شكّ فيه عند أهل الحقِّ، والقرآن العظيم مشحون به، وهو في السُّنَّة أكثر من أن يُحصى، خلافاً للمعتزلة ويجب قوله : (خلافاً للمعتزلة) : أي : حيث قالوا بعدم جواز الدُّعاء؛ محتجين بأنَّ ما قدَّره الله يكون فلا حاجة للدُّعاء، ويفسرون الدُّعاء المذكور في
His statement: ('The remover of blindness') means ignorance, and in his words there is an indication that supplication benefits, and that is indisputable among the people of truth, and the great Quran is full of it, and it is in the Sunnah more than can be counted, contrary to the Mu'tazila. And it is necessary. His statement: ('Contrary to the Mu'tazila'): i.e., they say that supplication is not permissible, arguing that what Allah has decreed will happen, so there is no need for supplication, and they interpret the supplication mentioned in...
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage comments on the phrase 'المزيل للعمى' (the remover of blindness), interpreting 'blindness' as ignorance. The commentator then notes that this implies supplication (دعاء) is beneficial, which is a certainty among Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., Ahl al-Sunna). He supports this by stating that the Quran is filled with supplication and the Sunnah contains more than can be counted. Then he mentions that the Mu'tazila hold the opposite view: they deny the permissibility of supplication, arguing that since everything is predestined by Allah, supplication is unnecessary and ineffective. The commentator's phrase 'ويجب' (and it is necessary) suggests that supplication is obligatory or required, but the sentence is cut off. The passage thus presents the Sunni position affirming the benefit of dua against the rationalist Mu'tazila objection based on predestination.

Key Terms
دعاء
معتزلة
أهل الحق
العمى (الجهل)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If everything is predestined, then supplication is useless and cannot change anything.
Correction: Supplication is itself predestined as a cause for good; it is part of the decree, not a challenge to it.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the position of the Mu'tazila regarding supplication?
A) They affirm its benefit
B) They consider it optional
C) They deny its permissibility
D) They require it

Key Takeaway
The benefit of supplication is a settled doctrine in Sunni Islam, supported by revelation and reason; the Mu'tazila denial is based on a flawed understanding of divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you genuinely believe that it can change your situation, or is it just a ritual? How does this lesson challenge any hidden doubt you may have about the effectiveness of supplication? Will you from now on make du'a with the conviction that it is a divinely ordained cause for benefit?
?
Have you ever stopped making du'a for something because you felt Allah already knows what's best? How does the refutation of the Mu'tazila in this passage expose the flaw in that reasoning? Will you commit to making du'a more consistently, trusting that it is part of Allah's decree to bring about good?
?
Think of a time when your du'a seemed unanswered. Did that lead you to question its value? After learning that supplication is a means ordained by Allah, how will you respond to future moments of perceived unanswered du'a? Will you persist in supplication with renewed faith?
